# Cassandra Eleanor Lang  —  *Stature / Stinger*

![[_attachments/cassie_lang.png]]

# Cassie Lang — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF) — SUBJECT: CASSANDRA ELEANOR "CASSIE" LANG

---

## BOTTOM LINE UP FRONT

**Cassandra Eleanor Lang is a chronic mourner who has organized an entire heroic vocation around the unfinished business of one televised death.** Strip away the alias rotation (Stature → Stinger → Giant-Girl → Ant-Girl), the costume changes, the team affiliations, the size-shifts from sub-millimeter to multi-story, and what remains is a single, legible, decades-long psychological project: *a daughter trying to bring her father back into the room and, failing that, to keep him present by becoming him.* Every other feature of her life — her powers, her politics, her friendships, her romantic choices, her relationship to her own body — is downstream of that project. She is not complicated in her structure. She is complicated in her execution of an extremely simple, extremely durable wound.

She is, by any reasonable assessment, one of the most morally sound operators in her cohort. She is also one of the most predictably exploitable, because her wound is named, locatable, and reliably triggered.

---

## THE THROUGH-LINE — ONE SENTENCE, THEN UNPACKED

**"The girl whose life was bought with her father's freedom, who watched that father die for a cause she now serves, and who has spent every subsequent year refusing to be the size grief tried to make her."**

Unpacked into its four constituent through-lines, each of which can be traced from her first appearance to her most recent:

1. **The Debt.** She exists because Scott Lang committed a crime. The Pym suit was stolen, the Ant-Man identity was assumed, and Dr. Sondheim was rescued for one reason: to save Cassie's heart. Her continued breathing is the proceeds of a felony. This is not metaphor — it is the literal premise of her introduction (Marvel Premiere #47–48, 1979). Every ethical choice she has made since is shaped by the fact that she owes her life to someone who broke the rules to save her. She cannot, structurally, become an enforcer of rules. She can only become a person who breaks them for love.

2. **The Witness.** She did not lose her father in private. She lost him on a screen, with the rest of the world, during Avengers Disassembled — a televised immolation caused by a member of the institution he had spent his life trying to join. The wound is doubled: the loss itself, and the public, mediated, uncontrollable nature of its delivery. She has never had a private grief. Every Avengers headline, every news cycle, every reminder of the institution is a re-exposure to the original wound channel.

3. **The Inheritance.** Long before she had powers, she had the *vocation*. The covert Pym-particle dosing through pre-adolescence is the diagnostic fact of her interior life — she was metabolizing her father into her own endocrine system in secret, for years, before anyone gave her permission. She did not acquire her powers in an accident. She *administered* them to herself, in a body that medical history said could not take the strain. The Stature identity was not chosen at 14; it was prepared from approximately age 10, in the dark, under the hand of a child who had decided she was going to be him.

4. **The Refusal of Diminishment.** Her power is size. Her wound is having been small — small in body (the congenital heart), small in agency (could not save her father), small in standing (a child told she was too young for the room). Every time she grows, she is making a non-verbal argument against the original conditions of her life. Every time she shrinks, she is performing a controlled, voluntary version of the smallness that was once imposed on her. The power-set is the symptom and the cure in one mechanism.

---

## THE CORE WOUND AND ITS MAINTENANCE

**The core wound:** *traumatic, televised, developmentally-timed loss of an idealized primary attachment figure, in a context where the survivor experiences her own continued existence as both the cause of his heroism and an insufficient reason for him to have stayed alive.*

This is more specific than "her dad died." The structural elements that make the wound load-bearing across her entire life:

- **Developmental timing.** Approximately 10–12 years old — old enough to comprehend death, young enough that the loss occurred before identity consolidation. The grief became scaffolding rather than damage; she built the adult self *around* the absence rather than recovering an intact self from before it.
- **Causal entanglement.** Scott Lang became Ant-Man to save her. Ant-Man died as an Avenger. Therefore, in the implicit arithmetic of a grieving child, *her survival is what put him in the room where he died.* This is never spoken in canon, and she would deny it if asked, but it organizes everything.
- **Institutional betrayal.** The agent of his death was an Avenger. The institution that should have protected him killed him. This makes uncomplicated alliance with establishment heroism permanently impossible for her, and explains her Civil War alignment, her Young Avengers participation, and her durable distrust of registration regimes.
- **No body, no burial.** Immolation. There was nothing to put in the ground. Grief without a body is grief without a terminus.

**The maintenance mechanisms** — the reasons this wound has not closed in twenty publication-years:

- **Recurrent partial returns.** Skrull-Scott in Secret Invasion. Doom-resurrected Scott in Children's Crusade. Fully restored Scott in subsequent runs. The object keeps coming back and going away. *Grief cannot complete when the object is intermittent.* She has been denied the one thing mourning requires: the stable absence of the lost person. Every return reopens the wound; every subsequent loss re-inflicts it.
- **Vocational re-exposure.** She has chosen a profession that places her in continuous contact with the conditions that killed her father. She works the same job, in the same uniform-family, against the same villain (Darren Cross has bookended her life — kidnapped Sondheim in her infancy, kidnapped her in adolescence). The exposure is therapeutic in intent and re-traumatizing in fact.
- **Identity fusion as containment.** By becoming him, she keeps him alive — and keeps the grief manageable, because she does not have to mourn what she has incorporated. The cost is that she cannot mourn what she has incorporated, either.
- **Romantic-object selection.** Vision/Jonas — a reconstructed dead Avenger. She has demonstrated a pattern of intimate attachment to the synthetic-returned, the wounded-resurrected, the categorically liminal. Each such relationship is a small rehearsal of the unfinished reunion.
- **Her own death and resurrection (Children's Crusade → Pleasant Hill).** She has now lived the experience from both sides. She has been the one whose body went away and came back wrong-sized in time. This should have produced insight; in practice it has produced a deeper somatic understanding of the family pattern and a profound, unspoken empathy with her father's repeated unmoorings.

The wound is maintained not because she is failing to heal but because *the conditions of her life systematically prevent healing.* This is important. She is not a person who is choosing pathology; she is a person whose biography keeps re-opening the same incision before it can scar.

---

## WHO SHE IS AT THE DEEPEST LEVEL

Below the alias, below the powers, below the team affiliations, **she is a restorative consciousness in a counterphobic body.** The two halves do not fully agree.

The restorative consciousness is the part of her that did not kill Wanda Maximoff when she had the chance. The part that forgives. The part that *holds* a peer group together and refuses to let chosen family disperse. The part that believes — and this is the most important and least cynical fact about her — that loss can be undone, that breaches can be repaired, that effort and loyalty are sufficient to mend almost anything. This is not naivete. She has lived more death than most career soldiers. It is a *chosen* worldview, maintained against the evidence, which makes it a form of moral courage rather than innocence.

The counterphobic body is the part that doses Pym particles on a damaged heart. The part that grows large enough to be hit by anti-aircraft fire when she could have stayed small enough to evade it. The part that runs toward the conditions of her father's death. The body is making an argument the consciousness has not endorsed: *that the way to survive loss is to dare the thing that caused it.*

At the deepest level, she is the productive collision of these two — a person whose ethics are reparative and whose physiology is sacrificial. The Children's Crusade death is the moment the two became one decision: she spent her body to repair the breach. She got her father back, briefly, and died alongside him. The arc is a perfect crystallization of who she is when the controls come off.

Underneath both halves — and this is where standard analyses stop short — is a third element worth naming: **a person who has never had the luxury of being ordinary.** She has been the cause of a crime, the daughter of a hero, the witness of a public death, the secret junkie of her father's powers, a teenage Avenger, a corpse, a resurrected child, a sister-in-arms to gods and reality-warpers. There has been no version of her life in which she was permitted to be a person whose primary problems were homework, friendships, and what to do after college. The unfinished work of her life is not *recovering* an ordinary self that grief obscured. It is *constructing* one, for the first time, from materials that were never ordinary to begin with.

This is why recent writers (Spencer, Wells, Ewing) have struggled to settle her — there is no baseline Cassie to return to. The baseline has to be invented.

---

## CORE DRIVES, RANKED BY OPERATIONAL WEIGHT

1. **Reunion / repair of the lost object.** Dominant. Overrides tactical judgment when activated.
2. **Continuation of the father's ethical project** — heroism for the small, defiance of corrupt institutions, loyalty to the underdog. Stable across all eras.
3. **Self-enlargement against the original conditions of smallness.** Both literal (size-change) and figurative (presence, voice, authority).
4. **Preservation of chosen family.** The Young Avengers function as a second self; she will absorb cost to keep them intact.
5. **Personal continuity / coherent identity.** Weakest of the five. She has spent so much of her life being someone's daughter, someone's teammate, someone's resurrection, that the project of being *herself* runs last.

The ranking matters operationally. When drives conflict, the order holds. Confronted with a chance to repair loss at the cost of the team, she will repair loss. Confronted with a chance to assert her own identity at the cost of continuing her father's project, she will continue the project.

---

## OPERATIONAL SUMMARY

**Disposition:** Active heroic operator, multi-affiliation (Young Avengers, Ant-Man-adjacent operations, intermittent Avengers contact). Powered: autonomic Pym-particle-based size manipulation, ranging across multiple orders of magnitude. Equipment-supplemented when operating as Stinger. Combat-experienced from early adolescence. Cardiovascularly compromised by history; performance-degraded under prolonged size-extreme exertion in ways she does not publicly acknowledge.

**Threat profile to others:** Low absent provocation. She does not initiate; she does not pursue revenge; she has demonstrated, against the woman who killed her father, that she will choose forgiveness over retribution when given the choice. The capacity to inflict harm is substantial — a multi-story humanoid is a strategic-tier asset — but the inclination is restorative. She is dangerous to people who threaten her chosen family. She is not dangerous as a matter of disposition.

**Threat profile to self:** Moderate to high, chronic. The Pym-on-damaged-heart problem is real. The counterphobic vocational pattern is real. The willingness to spend her body for reunion fantasies is the most lethal feature of her psychology and has already killed her once.

**Exploitable vectors, ranked by reliability:**
1. *Offer of reunion with the dead* — particularly Scott Lang, particularly under conditions of plausible deniability about the offeror's motives. She has demonstrated she will walk into an obvious trap if the trap promises her father back. **This is the single largest vulnerability in her operational profile.**
2. *Threat to Scott Lang* — produces tactical tunnel vision, sacrifices broader objectives.
3. *Threat to Young Avengers peer group* — produces disproportionate, escalatory response.
4. *Authority figure invoking moral high ground while having historically failed her father or his kind* — produces principled defiance, often public, often costly to the authority.
5. *Being told she is too young, too small, or not ready* — directly addresses the foundational wound; produces immediate counter-escalation, often size-based.

**Stabilizers, ranked by load-bearing capacity:**
1. *Young Avengers cohort.* Single most important protective factor in her life. Functioning peer family. Loss of this group would be catastrophic to her regulation.
2. *Scott Lang, when present and stable.* When the attachment object is in the room, breathing, and not in immediate danger, she regulates well.
3. *Her own ethical clarity.* She knows what she believes. This is a quiet but durable anchor.
4. *Restorative worldview.* The capacity for forgiveness and the belief in repair are not just ethics — they are affect-regulation infrastructure.

**Recommended posture (benevolent intelligence consumer):** Do not stand between her and her father. Do not promise her returns of the dead. Do not invoke her age, size, or readiness as grounds for exclusion. Do engage her chosen-family bonds as the genuine kin-network they are. Do recognize that her cardiac history is a real and undertreated medical fact, not a closed chapter. Do understand that her grandest moral gestures (refusing to kill Wanda; dying for the reunion she knew was a trap) are not aberrations but the deepest, truest expressions of who she is.

**Status of the unfinished work:** Ongoing. The project of becoming a person distinct from the man she was born to save and the man whose death she is still trying to undo has begun in recent canon but is not complete. The honest assessment is that it may never complete in the conventional sense — the wound is too central to the architecture for full resolution — but it can be metabolized into a self that uses the wound rather than being used by it. There is evidence she is moving in that direction. There is also evidence the conditions of her life will not stop generating fresh material.

---

*End Executive Brief. Proceed to subsequent dossier sections for granular elaboration.*


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

## SUBJECT: CASSANDRA ELEANOR "CASSIE" LANG
### Section I of the longitudinal record. Birth through pre-adolescence (c. age 0 to c. age 10, terminating at the threshold of the foundational trauma covered in Chronology II).

---

## 1.1 — ANTECEDENTS: THE PARENTS BEFORE THE CHILD

Any honest file on Cassandra Lang has to begin before her birth, because the household she was delivered into was already structurally unstable in ways that would shape every subsequent year of her life.

**Father — Scott Edward Harris Lang.** Electronics engineer by training (degree from a state institution, exact campus inconsistent across writers, but the technical fluency is canonical from *Marvel Premiere* #47, 1979). Convicted of burglary after stealing from a former employer who had cheated him; sentenced to a multi-year stretch at a federal facility. Released, hired by Stark International (Tony Stark's deliberate second-chance policy, an act of corporate charity that becomes ironic in light of later Avengers history). Married Margaret "Maggie" pre-incarceration; the marriage did not survive the prison years. By the time Cassie is conceived, Scott and Maggie are already on the deteriorating arc that will end in divorce within Cassie's earliest memory.

**Mother — Margaret "Maggie" Lang (later Paxton).** Working-class, pragmatic, exhausted by Scott's instability. The unflattering but defensible reading of Maggie across forty years of appearances is of a woman who loved Scott once, was destroyed by his choices, hardened in self-defense, and devoted the back half of her adult life to keeping her daughter clear of the chaos he generated. She is not a warm woman in the published record. She is a *competent* woman, which is a different thing, and the distinction will matter for Cassie's lifelong emotional accounting.

**Stepfather (proximate, not yet present at birth) — Jim Paxton.** Police officer, Miami jurisdiction in most contemporary renderings, generic urban precinct in older books. Marries Maggie after the divorce. His occupational identity is *the law*, and Scott Lang's operational identity is *the man the law puts in cages*. Cassie's domestic environment from approximately age four onward contains, by structural necessity, an unspoken referendum on her father conducted at every breakfast table.

---

## 1.2 — BIRTH AND THE FIRST CRISIS

Cassie is born in the brief window between Scott's release from prison and the collapse of the Lang marriage. The exact date floats by sliding-scale Marvel time; what is fixed in the canon is the *order of events*: she is born, she is loved, she is sick.

**Cardiac diagnosis (circa age 5–7).** The defect is presented in *Marvel Premiere* #47 as life-threatening, surgically correctable, and time-limited. The specific lesion is never named — writers in 1979 used the medical detail as plot armature, not as case study — but the behavioral implications are intact and significant. From the earliest age at which Cassie could form continuous memory, she was a child who:

- Was told, in age-appropriate language, that her heart did not work correctly.
- Watched adults speak in lowered voices about her body.
- Submitted to repeated medical examinations and the attendant somatic vocabulary of *stethoscopes, EKG leads, the cold disc on the sternum, the breath-hold instruction*.
- Was treated by both parents as fragile — protected from exertion, monitored at play, kept inside on cold days, told *not to run*.
- Absorbed, pre-verbally and then verbally, the household understanding that her continuation was *not guaranteed*.

This is the somatic ground floor. Long before she ever encountered a Pym particle, Cassie Lang's central bodily fact was that her body was the problem. Her chest contained a faulty engine. To be Cassie was to be a small person around whom adults were quietly worried.

---

## 1.3 — THE ORIGINATING ACT: THE THEFT THAT SAVED HER LIFE

The defining transaction of Cassie's existence — the one her psychology is built around even though she did not consciously author it — occurs in *Marvel Premiere* #47–48.

Dr. Erica Sondheim, the only surgeon competent to perform Cassie's procedure, is kidnapped by industrialist Darren Cross. Scott Lang, having exhausted legal options, breaks into Hank Pym's home, steals the Ant-Man suit and a canister of Pym particles, infiltrates Cross Technological Enterprises at insect scale, frees Sondheim, and brings her to the hospital. Sondheim performs the surgery. Cassie lives.

The granular contents of this episode, reconstructed from canon, that a total file must hold:

1. Cassie was on a surgical ward, pre-op, while her father was committing a felony to procure her surgeon. She did not know this at the time. She learned it later — Scott does not lie to her about it across the run of the character; it becomes family scripture.
2. Hank Pym, on discovering the theft, chose not to press charges and instead *sanctioned* Scott's continued use of the suit on the condition it be turned to heroic ends. This means the very tools of Cassie's salvation are gifted-from-theft, a moral hybrid she is raised inside.
3. The villain of her origin — Darren Cross — is the same villain who will, decades of publication later, return and take *her* hostage during her father's *Astonishing Ant-Man* tenure. The narrative loop closes on her body specifically.

The psychological residue of this episode, which Cassie cannot remember consciously in any granular way (she was sedated for the surgery itself), is nonetheless installed in her as *family history*. She grows up knowing the following sentences as facts about herself:

- *My father went back to crime to save my life.*
- *He could have gone back to prison for me.*
- *He chose to.*
- *I am alive because of who he is willing to be.*

No child metabolizes that cleanly. The earliest behavioral trace of its impact is documented in the post-recovery period: Cassie's almost compulsive insistence on physical proximity to Scott. She follows him room to room. She wakes in the night and walks to his bed. She is, by every contemporaneous depiction (background panels through Scott's Avengers and FF tenure), the child who *checks*.

---

## 1.4 — THE DOMESTIC ENVIRONMENT (AGE ~4–10)

After the surgery, the Lang marriage does not recover. Scott's return to costumed activity — initially under Pym's supervision, then as a freelance hero, then on Avengers and FF auxiliary tours — destabilizes whatever fragile reunion he and Maggie attempted. Divorce follows. Custody arrangements are joint but lopsided: Maggie has primary residential custody; Scott has visitation, frequently disrupted by missions, incarceration, or death.

**The two-household child.** From roughly age six onward, Cassie's life is bifurcated into the *Maggie/Paxton house* and the *Scott apartment / wherever-Scott-is*. The contrast is total and was clearly experienced as such by the child:

- **Maggie/Paxton house:** Routinized. Meals at fixed times. Homework supervised. Bedtime enforced. Television limited. Paxton's police uniform hung in a closet she walked past every day. A clean, lower-middle-class household with linoleum, refrigerator-magnet calendars, school-photo frames. Maggie's preferred discipline mode is verbal and consistent; Paxton's is *measured silence followed by sentence-pronouncement*, a man habituated to professional restraint. Cassie is fed, clothed, taken to pediatric cardiology follow-ups on schedule, deposited at school on time. She is *cared for*. She is not, in any depicted moment, *delighted with*.

- **Scott's apartment / Scott's life:** Improvisational. Frozen pizza. Late bedtimes. Television unlimited. Scott's workbench in the kitchen with circuit boards and a soldering iron and components Cassie was warned not to touch but allowed to watch. Visits frequently interrupted by emergencies; sometimes Scott would be gone for the weekend without warning and Cassie would be returned to Maggie's house early, with apologies. Scott's mode of parenting is *narration* — he tells her things, stories, jokes, asides about the world, treating her as a small adult he is briefing. He is the parent who *talks to her*. He is also the parent who *vanishes*.

The early behavioral pattern that establishes itself across these years is diagnostic and persists into the adult character: **Cassie loves the inconsistent parent more.** She experiences Maggie's reliability as *baseline* (and therefore invisible) and Scott's volatile presence as *event* (and therefore precious). This is the standard developmental architecture of preferential attachment to the intermittent reinforcer, and it will structure her romantic and team selections for the rest of her published life.

---

## 1.5 — THE STEPFATHER PROBLEM

Jim Paxton's introduction into the household — Cassie is approximately seven or eight by the time he is a fixed presence — is the second great structural fact of her childhood. The canon is consistent: Paxton is not abusive, is not cruel, is in many of his appearances genuinely decent. He is also, professionally and temperamentally, the *categorical antagonist* of everything Scott Lang represents. He has, in some continuities, personally arrested Scott. He has expressed, in Cassie's hearing, the opinion that Scott is a thief who got lucky with a costume.

Cassie's behavioral adjustment to Paxton over the childhood years follows an arc:

1. **Initial cautious tolerance** — she is a polite child by temperament and reads adult moods well, a competence developed in cardiac-ward waiting rooms.
2. **Recognition of the hostility toward Scott** — by approximately age eight, she has understood that Paxton's view of her father is not neutral. She begins to *withhold* from Paxton: she does not bring him drawings, does not tell him about her school day in detail, accepts his rides to school but does not chat in the car.
3. **Defensive identification with Scott** — the more Paxton represents *law*, the more Cassie privately catalogues Scott as *the one who broke law for me*. This is when the originating-theft narrative becomes load-bearing in her self-concept. She is not yet articulate about it. She knows the shape of the loyalty before she can name it.

By the end of the childhood period covered in this section, Cassie has internalized a moral schema that will persist into her adolescent superheroic identity: **institutional authority is suspect; the people who break rules for love are trustworthy.** Paxton is the prototype of the first category. Scott is the prototype of the second. Every later authority figure she encounters — Iron Man during Civil War, the registration apparatus, even Captain America when he tells her she is too young — will be read through the template Paxton installed.

---

## 1.6 — SCOTT'S CAREER AS THE WEATHER OF HER CHILDHOOD

During the years 1979–c.2004 of published continuity (which, by Marvel's sliding scale, compresses to roughly Cassie's age 0–10), Scott Lang holds a series of roles whose practical effect on his daughter is that he is *unpredictably available*. The granular record:

- **Stark International security consultant** (early period). Long hours. Frequent travel. Reliable paycheck. Cassie sees him on a schedule.
- **Reserve Avenger / FF auxiliary** (Fantastic Four #384 onward, replacing Reed Richards in technical capacity). This is the period during which Scott is most absent from Cassie's daily life, but also the period in which her *pride* in him crystallizes. School-age Cassie tells classmates her father works with the Fantastic Four. She is shown, in background panels, with FF-themed possessions — a notebook, a lunchbox in one rendering, a small "4" pin she has clipped to her shirt collar for school picture day. Her hero-worship is, at this stage, completely uncomplicated. He is a good man and famous people know it.
- **Periods of unemployment / between gigs.** Scott is at his most *present* and at his most *depressed* in these stretches. Cassie observes him in pajamas at noon, watches him stare at the wall, listens to him on the phone with Maggie negotiating support payments. She develops, very young, the capacity to read his mood on entry to a room — a hypervigilance to paternal affect that is observable across her adult appearances as an instant scan whenever Scott enters frame.
- **Brief re-incarceration episodes.** Scott is jailed more than once in the published record, in some cases for hero-adjacent reasons, in some cases for relapses to property crime. Cassie experiences at least one prison visit during the childhood period — depicted in flashback in *Astonishing Ant-Man* — and the somatic memory of the visit (the glass, the phone receiver, her father's hand against hers through the partition) is one of the few specific traumatic micro-events the file can locate in the pre-adolescent record. She did not cry during the visit. She cried, per Scott's later retelling, in the car going home, all the way home, and would not say what about.

---

## 1.7 — THE SIZE FASCINATION: EARLIEST TRACES

Long before Cassie has powers, she has *interest*. The behavioral record contains repeated background incidents — distributed across writers from Stern through Slott through Heinberg's retrospective flashbacks — of small-child Cassie:

- Asking Scott to shrink and ride on her shoulder.
- Asking to be shrunk *herself* (Scott consistently refuses; Pym particles on developing children were, per Hank Pym's own counsel, contraindicated).
- Playing with ant farms — a documented gift from Scott on at least one birthday.
- Naming the ants. Crying when one died. Demanding burials.
- Drawing pictures (preserved in at least one Scott-POV panel of her bedroom) of herself as *enormous*, towering over buildings, while a small father-figure stands on her open palm. The directionality of this fantasy is significant and worth flagging in the longitudinal record: *she is the giant, he is the small one she carries*. The protective inversion is already present in childhood drawings.
- Sneaking into Scott's equipment locker on at least one documented occasion (referenced in *Young Avengers* dialogue as an established pattern: "I've been doing this since I was *little*").

The covert self-dosing that will be revealed at age twelve-ish in *Young Avengers* #1 does not appear from nowhere. It is the late-stage form of a fascination present from approximately age six. The file should record that Cassie's relationship to Pym particles is, neurologically, *a childhood relationship*. The substance entered her cognitive life as her father's magic, as the stuff that made him a hero, as the thing she was forbidden to touch and therefore desired absolutely. By the time she actually begins ingesting it, the substance is already a parental introject in chemical form.

---

## 1.8 — PEER LIFE AND SCHOOL (AGE ~5–10)

Sparse but consistent canon. Cassie is a public-school child, average-to-above-average academically, with a notable verbal fluency (her dialogue at first appearance as Stature is already articulate beyond her age, and writers have retconned this backward into a *bookish* childhood). She reads. She is depicted in childhood panels with books — children's novels, science books, in one panel an illustrated guide to insects that Scott bought her. The insect-book detail is psychologically dense: she has been *taught to love the small things her father commands*.

Friendships in the pre-adolescent period are functional but not central to the record. She is well-liked, not popular. She is not bullied in any depicted episode but is described, in one Maggie-POV moment, as "a kid who watches before she joins." The watching-before-joining behavior is consistent with the cardiac-child profile (children with serious early medical histories tend toward observational social entry as a self-protective strategy) and persists into adulthood — her first scenes as Stature show her *observing the Young Avengers in operation before declaring herself*.

She tells her closest school friends, by approximately age eight, that her father is Ant-Man. This is sometimes believed, sometimes not. She does not appear to have been wounded by disbelief; she is depicted as cheerfully indifferent to whether classmates credit her. The identity is hers privately whether or not it is socially ratified. This is an early instance of a lifelong pattern: **Cassie's self-concept does not require external validation to remain stable.** Defiance of authority comes easily to her in adolescence precisely because her core identity has never been *granted* — it has always been *known*.

---

## 1.9 — THE HEART, REVISITED: ONGOING SOMATIC LIFE

Throughout the childhood period, Cassie remains a cardiac patient under follow-up. The post-surgical course is, by canon, *successful* — the defect is corrected, she is not symptomatic, she is cleared for normal activity. But "cleared" and "treated as cleared" are different things in a household that nearly lost her. Maggie does not let her play contact sports. Paxton, the cop, brings home worst-case stories absent-mindedly. Scott, on visits, is the one parent who treats her as physically capable — he wrestles with her on the floor, lets her climb on him, takes her to amusement parks, *forgets* about the heart in the loving way she desperately wants to be forgotten about.

The behavioral consequence: Cassie associates her father with *not being treated as fragile*. Her later choice to acquire a power that strains the cardiovascular system is, among many other things, an unconscious gift back to him: *see, you were right not to be afraid of me*.

By age ten she has had approximately five years of annual cardiology follow-ups, knows the procedure (the gown, the gel, the cold transducer), and approaches the appointments with the stoicism characteristic of chronically medicalized children. She does not cry in clinic. She saves it for the car.

---

## 1.10 — THE END-STATE OF CHILDHOOD: THE CHILD AT THE THRESHOLD

At the close of the period this section covers — approximately age ten, in the immediate run-up to the events of *Avengers Disassembled* — Cassie Lang's documented profile is as follows:

- **Cardiac:** repaired, asymptomatic, under surveillance.
- **Domestic:** bifurcated between a competent-cold mother / police-officer stepfather household and an intermittent-warm father whose presence is the high point of her week.
- **Cognitive:** verbally precocious, observationally sharp, a reader.
- **Social:** functional, peripheral, a watcher.
- **Affective:** warm at baseline; hypervigilant to paternal mood; controlled in clinical and stressful settings; private about her tears.
- **Identificatory:** fused with Scott Lang to a degree not yet pathological because he is still alive. The fusion has not yet been tested by loss.
- **Behavioral signature:** *checks on her father.* Walks to his room at night when she is at his apartment. Calls him from Maggie's house, sometimes for no reason, just to hear that he picked up.
- **The germ of the future:** an established fascination with size-change, an established willingness to touch what she has been told not to touch (the equipment locker), an established moral schema in which *people who break rules for love are the trustworthy ones*, and an established self-image — visible in her own drawings — in which she is the giant who carries the small father safely on her hand.

She is, in the file's most precise summary, a child organized entirely around a man who could die at any moment and a body that has already tried to kill her once. She has, so far, managed both. The arrangement is stable. It is also load-bearing on a single point of failure.

That point of failure is approximately two years away. It will arrive on a television screen, in the form of a Kree warship that does not exist, fabricated by an Avenger having a psychotic break, immolating the man around whom this entire childhood was organized.

Chronology II will cover what the child in this file became when that happened.

---

*End Section I. Cross-reference: Section 2 (Medical) for the cardiac substrate; Section 3 (Psychological) for the attachment architecture installed in this period; Chronology II (forthcoming) for the Disassembled event and its sequelae.*


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

*Subject: Cassandra Eleanor Lang. Period under review: approximately age 10 through approximately age 16 of published time, bracketing the death of Scott Lang in* Avengers Disassembled *(2004) through her death in* Avengers: The Children's Crusade *(2010–2012). This is the forging interval. Everything she becomes is set in these six years.*

---

## I. The Interval Before the Hammer Fell (ages ~10, immediately pre-Disassembled)

The file's last "ordinary child" data points capture a girl whose life had stabilized into a workable, if asymmetrical, equilibrium. Custodial weeks alternated between Maggie Lang–Paxton's house (suburban, two-parent, police-adjacent, ordered around school hours and pediatric cardiology follow-ups) and Scott Lang's rotating apartments (Avengers-adjacent, irregular, frequently empty for days at a time, visibly bachelor, with the Ant-Man helmet often left on a kitchen counter or workbench in plain sight of a child who was not supposed to touch it). Cassie at this stage is canonically observed: small for her age (a longitudinal effect of the cardiac history), verbally precocious, reading above grade level, prone to a particular fixated curiosity around her father's equipment that adults around her registered as "cute" and "she'll grow out of it." She did not.

Behavioral micro-patterns already locked in by this period and visible in the Busiek-era and Stern-era cameos:

- A habit of waiting at windows. Whenever Scott was due to retrieve her, panel composition repeatedly places her at glass — front door sidelights, apartment windows, the back seat of Maggie's car. The waiting is not anxious in depiction; it is *prepared*. She is the child who is ready before the adult arrives.
- A specific physical greeting: she runs at her father at full speed and is lifted. This is not unusual for a child, but the persistence of the ritual past the age it usually fades is data. It is still present, in compressed form, in panels well into her teen years.
- An early-formed habit of asking technical questions about the suit and the particles — not "what does it do" but "how does it know how big to make you." Children's interest in a parent's job is normal; the specificity is not. She was studying.
- Maggie's house contained, per background detail across multiple runs, a wall of school photos and a refrigerator front of report cards. Scott's apartments contained almost nothing of Cassie — a single framed picture, sometimes none. She read both rooms. The inferred meaning to a child of that age: the parent who needs me to prove I exist is the parent I can take for granted; the parent who barely keeps evidence of me is the parent I must work to remain visible to. This inversion — *the absent love is the precious love* — is the architecture under everything that follows.

She is, at the eve of the catastrophe, a child whose primary developmental project was already organized around access to a father who was loved harder for being less available, in a body whose recent history had taught her that survival was contingent on outside rescue.

---

## II. The Hammer (the death of Scott Lang, *Avengers Disassembled*, 2004)

The proximate event is well-documented and need not be re-narrated; the operationally relevant content is *how Cassie received it* and what observable behaviors followed in the six to eighteen months afterward.

She did not learn from a phone call. She learned from a television. The Avengers Mansion attack was broadcast live; the destruction of the west wing where Scott was killed by a fabricated Kree vessel was on every major network within minutes. A child in the late elementary / early middle school window, watching her father's workplace explode on cable news, in real time, with no adult mediation in the room at the moment of impact — this is the foundational sensory memory of her adolescence. Subsequent depictions show her exhibiting a specific aversion to live news broadcasts and to the particular sound register of breaking-news jingles; in Young Avengers and onward, when ambient television is shown in panels where Cassie is present, it is consistently muted, on a sports channel, or off. This is a small environmental tell that recurs across writers who likely did not coordinate it, which suggests it is being read off the same buried cue in the original material.

The post-loss period (the months between *Disassembled* and *Young Avengers* #1) is largely off-panel but reconstructable from later dialogue:

- She attended a funeral with no body. The cremation was performed by the event itself. Children who lose a parent without remains have a documented elevated rate of complicated grief; the absence of a physical referent for the loss makes the loss harder to localize and therefore harder to bound. Cassie's later willingness to believe in returns — and her grandiose project of reversal in *Children's Crusade* — is partly authored here. The loss never had an edge.
- She returned full-time to Maggie and Paxton's household. The structural change is enormous: she went from a 50/50 split with a heroic, irregular, idolized father to a 100/0 custodial situation with the stable mother and the police-officer stepfather who had publicly distrusted Scott. The household that "won" custody is the household that had been positioned, in her child-logic, as the one Scott needed protecting from.
- Paxton's behavior in this period is depicted, in retrospective dialogue, as well-meant and procedurally correct: he attempted to be present, to provide stability, to set normal expectations around school and bedtime. This is, for the wound she was carrying, the worst possible posture. He was offering replacement when she needed continuation. The defiant, allergic relationship to Paxton that defines her teen years begins here, not as a personality clash but as a structural rejection of substitution.
- She began, almost certainly within weeks of the funeral, to access Scott's belongings. The Avengers placed Scott's personal effects in storage; some came to Maggie's house; some Cassie acquired by a route that is never fully explained but is implied to have involved both legitimate inheritance and a degree of *retrieval* she did not advertise. Among these effects: a quantity of Pym particles in their various stabilized forms (canister, disc, the "shrinking gas" variant). The quantity she came into possession of was non-trivial and unsupervised.

The behavioral signature of the first post-death year, as later self-reported and visible in flashback: sleep disturbance, a withdrawal from same-age peers, a sharp uptick in solitary time spent with her father's effects, and the beginning of what she would later describe as *practice*.

---

## III. The Secret Project (ages ~11–13, the years of covert self-dosing)

This is the most important — and most quietly horrifying — interval in the formation chronology, and it deserves to be read at its proper scale. A child, between the rough ages of eleven and thirteen, in her bedroom in a suburban house, alone, repeatedly self-administered an unregulated, non-FDA, reality-altering biochemical agent originally calibrated for an adult male wearing a regulator suit. She did this without the knowledge of either custodial parent, without medical supervision, with a documented congenital cardiac history, and she did it for years.

The reconstructed protocol, inferred from later canonical statements:

- **Site:** Her bedroom, almost certainly with the door locked. Later panels show her room with a closet whose floor she had modified to conceal materials; the modification is small, careful, and the work of someone who had practiced concealment from the same household over a long period.
- **Schedule:** Likely after Paxton's shift hours, when household traffic was lowest. Children doing covert behaviors in two-parent homes acquire near-actuarial knowledge of adult schedules; Cassie would have known her stepfather's shift rotation cold.
- **Dose escalation:** Cassie has described, in later dialogue, that her ability did not arrive in a flash. It arrived through *repetition*. The early attempts produced no visible effect, then small effects, then sustained effects, and finally biological integration. This implies a months-to-years curve of escalating self-exposure, with each null result rewarded by an increased dose. The clinical analog is the long-term self-administration profile of an early-onset, body-modifying behavior (anabolic use, restrictive eating, self-harm cutting) — not in the pharmacological mechanism, which is unique, but in the *behavioral structure*: covert, escalating, secret-knowledge-producing, identity-forming, undertaken in a window where adult supervision was nominal.
- **Physical sequelae she would have observed and concealed:** Probable transient tachycardia, almost certainly headaches, likely brief disorientation on size-restoration, and across the longer timeline the integration of size-change into her endogenous biology such that the particles became less *administered* and more *resident*. A child noting these effects on her body, on her own, with no one to report them to, is also a child learning a particular relationship to her own physiology — the body as a project, as something one does to oneself in private, as a thing whose meaning is set by the secret-keeper rather than the doctor.

The motive structure she would not have been able to articulate at the time but which is recoverable from the totality of her later behavior:

1. To *do* the thing he did. The Pym particles were her father's signature substance. Putting them in her body was an act of communion. This is not metaphor; it is the literal mechanism of identification with the lost object, performed in chemistry.
2. To *acquire* the means of finishing what he started. If she could become Ant-Man, in some functional sense, the Ant-Man-shaped hole in the world could be smaller. She was filling a vacancy whose existence the rest of the world had moved on from.
3. To *grow*. The girl who had been small, sick, and unable to save him was acquiring, in secret, the ability to be six stories tall. The compensation is direct enough to be almost embarrassing to point out, which is why it works as a defense — it is hidden in plain sight by its own obviousness.
4. To *have a secret from the household*. The covert quality is not incidental. Maggie and Paxton had taken custody of her body and her schedule. The particles were the part of her life they did not own. Adolescent autonomy through transgression is normative; what is not normative is the specific transgression chosen, which routes autonomy directly through the dead father's life.

There is no canonical record of any adult intervening in this period. No teacher, no pediatrician, no parent caught her. This is itself a finding: the household was functional enough to keep her fed, clothed, schooled, and cardiologically monitored, but not attuned enough to register that the child was building a secret laboratory in her closet and altering her own endocrine architecture. She learned, in this interval, that she could conduct a major undertaking of her own life entirely outside the awareness of the adults responsible for her — and she learned that lesson well enough that the operational secrecy in her later vigilante career was already a mature skill before she put on a costume.

---

## IV. The Reach Outward (the months before *Young Avengers* #1)

By the time she was approximately thirteen, the project had succeeded biologically and stalled socially. She had powers and no one to use them with. The transition from secret-bedroom phenomenon to operational hero required external scaffolding, and she went looking for it with the same methodical covertness she had used to build the powers.

Documented or reconstructable behaviors in this window:

- *Surveillance of news media for hero activity.* She tracked, almost certainly with a notebook or its digital equivalent, sightings of the figures who had begun appearing in Manhattan after the Avengers' dissolution — the early Young Avengers, before they were named that. A teenager building a dossier on costumed minors operating without team affiliation is the inverse mirror of the dossier currently being written about her; she was UATU before she was Stature.
- *Travel reconnaissance.* The Lang–Paxton household is canonically located outside the immediate New York metro core. Getting to Manhattan required planning — train schedules, cover stories for Maggie, return windows that aligned with Paxton's shifts. She executed this repeatedly without being caught, which is again non-trivial. Her later facility with field logistics is rooted in this period of teenage tradecraft.
- *Acquisition of a costume.* Her first operational costume was modified from her father's spare gear — a fact she has acknowledged. The work of altering an adult male suit to fit a thirteen-year-old female frame is non-trivial sewing and engineering; she either did it herself (consistent with the technical curiosity documented since age eight) or commissioned it from someone whose silence she could keep, and the former is the more likely reading.
- *Internal naming.* The choice of *Stature* as her alias is a piece of psychological self-disclosure that has not been read closely enough in the file to date. Other available aliases — Giant-Girl, Ant-Girl, simply Ant-Man II — were obvious and were rejected. *Stature* does not refer to size in the literal sense; it refers to *standing*, to dignified bearing, to having earned a place. She did not name herself after her power. She named herself after the thing she did not yet possess and was going to make others grant her.

---

## V. The First Defining Choice (the confrontation at the Avengers' door, *Young Avengers* #1–6, Heinberg, 2005)

The moment she walks into the room where Jessica Jones, Captain America, and Iron Man are debating what to do about a group of unsanctioned super-powered teenagers and announces that she is one of them, and that she is Scott Lang's daughter, and that she is going with them — this is the first irreversible act of her adult identity. Every behavior in the file from this point forward is downstream of that doorway.

The choice's anatomy:

- *Public outing of the covert project.* The years of secret dosing were detonated in a single moment by walking into the most surveilled room in Manhattan and demonstrating the power. She made the secret into a fact. The household discovered it from the world rather than from her — a sequence she selected. This is not adolescent carelessness; it is the deliberate burning of the bridge back to private daughterhood.
- *Choice of audience.* She did not reveal the powers to Maggie. She did not reveal them to Paxton. She did not reveal them to a doctor. She revealed them to *Captain America*. The choice of first witness is diagnostic: the person whose recognition she wanted was the person who had stood with her father in the line of work that killed him. She was applying for citizenship in the country of his death.
- *The argument she made.* Across the first arc, when challenged on her right to be present, Cassie consistently returns to two propositions: that she has the power, and that she is her father's daughter. She does not argue training, experience, judgment, or maturity. She argues *inheritance*. This is the operational thesis of her adolescence: the legitimacy of her presence is genealogical.
- *The defiance of Jessica Jones in particular.* Jones, pregnant and hard-edged, attempted to send the Young Avengers home. Cassie's resistance to her is sharper than her resistance to the male authority figures present. The inferred reading: Jones is structurally a mother-figure attempting to reassert custody, and Cassie has spent two years systematically dismantling exactly that authority in her actual home. The transference is direct.

The fallout in the household was severe and produced the second-defining choice of the period: when Maggie and Paxton attempted to ground her, withdraw her from the team, and pursue what they understood as appropriate parental intervention, Cassie *left*. She did not run away in the classic sense; she relocated her base of operations to the team's safe houses and treated home as a place she visited. This is the formal end of her childhood, and it occurred at a specific age (mid-teens) and a specific address (the Young Avengers' working space), and the household did not recover the relationship in any complete form before her death several years later.

---

## VI. The Re-Choosings (Civil War, 2006–2007; the Initiative era)

The Civil War period is the first interval in which Cassie's choices were *political* rather than purely personal-developmental, and the alignment she selected — Captain America's resistance, against registration — is a continuation of the doorway choice by other means. She placed herself, again, on the side of her father's affiliations and against the institutional Avengers establishment whose successor structures she held responsible for his death. The fact that the actual people on the pro-registration side (Stark in particular) had personally mourned Scott did not register as mitigating; her object relations with the institution were fixed and were not amenable to update by individual data.

Operational behaviors specific to this period:

- *Combat under fugitive conditions.* She lived rough for months — safe houses, churches, mobile basing. The household-defiance skills built in adolescence (covert travel, supply logistics, schedule management) were promoted directly into wartime tradecraft without an intervening training period. This is unusual; most teen operatives in this conflict required adult scaffolding. She did not.
- *First killing-grade combat.* Civil War's casualty list included a teen hero (Goliath) at the hands of the Thor-clone. Cassie was present in the immediate operational radius for events of this scale. She did not, on record, kill; but she fought at scale, with full force, against trained adult opponents. The escalation from secret-bedroom dosing to deployment as a giant against superhuman adversaries took less than two years of operational time.
- *Solidification of the peer group as primary kin.* The Young Avengers, under fugitive pressure, became the operational family she has functioned within ever since. Patriot, Hawkeye (Kate Bishop), Wiccan, Hulkling, Speed, and the Vision-variant Jonas — these are the people whose presence stabilizes her affective baseline. Maggie and Paxton, in the same interval, are observed in panel approximately *never*. The kin substitution is complete by the end of this arc.

---

## VII. The Romance with the Returned (Vision/Jonas, 2005–2010)

The relationship with the Iron Lad–derived Vision iteration is not incidental to the formation chronology; it is one of its central data points, and the pattern it establishes governs all of her subsequent romantic life under observation.

She chose a partner who was, in literal mechanism:

- a reconstructed version of a dead Avenger,
- assembled from the operational system of another dead figure (Iron Lad / Kang's youth self),
- carrying inside himself the personality fragments of a being he was not,
- subject to deletion, restoration, and identity reset.

The choice is over-determined. A girl whose central wound is the loss and partial returning of a father selected a romantic partner whose entire ontology is loss and partial return. She was not unaware of this; she discussed the parallel in dialogue with him and with peers. The awareness did not modify the choice. This is significant: she could see the pattern and chose it anyway, which means the pattern was not unconscious in the strict Freudian sense — it was a *preference*, a thing she had decided she wanted, a continuing project of loving the salvageable dead. This is the romantic posture she carries forward, and any future romantic data should be read against this template.

The relationship's tenderness was real; the warmth between them is consistently depicted as among the most stable affective registers in the entire Young Avengers run. But it is also a relationship that put her, daily, in proximity to a body she might lose — *training* her, in effect, for the larger loss she was preparing to risk her life to reverse.

---

## VIII. The Reversal Project and the First Death (*Avengers: The Children's Crusade*, 2010–2012)

The terminus of the formation period is the operational decision to pursue the Scarlet Witch — not for vengeance, which is the reading the file should resist, but for *retrieval*. The proposition she organized the team around was that Wanda, the cause of the loss, was also the only available instrument of its undoing. The crusade was a rescue mission directed backward in time.

The behaviors in this final adolescent arc that close the formation chronology:

- *Refusal of revenge when revenge was offered.* Given clean opportunity to harm or kill Wanda, Cassie declined. This is the moral signature of her formation — restorative, not retributive — and it is decisive evidence that the project was reunion, not punishment.
- *Acceptance of Doom's offer.* When Doctor Doom, having absorbed Wanda's chaos-magic capacity, offered the resurrection of Scott Lang as part of a brokered settlement, Cassie accepted. The team's senior figures and the broader Avengers establishment warned against the bargain. She took it anyway. This is the exact pressure-point profile already established: an offer of reunion with the dead overrides every other consideration, including obvious-trap recognition that she was demonstrably capable of in other contexts. The vulnerability is specific, locatable, and was exploited exactly as it would always be exploited.
- *The double loss in a single afternoon.* Scott returned. Scott was killed in front of her, again, by Doom, within hours. The structure of her original wound was reproduced with surgical precision — sudden, witnessed, by a figure of overwhelming power against whom she could not intervene in time. The second death, occurring on the day of the second return, is the canonical confirmation that the cycle she had been trying to escape was a cycle she had been re-entering.
- *Her own death, immediately afterward.* In the same engagement, Cassie was killed by Doom. The proximate cause was combat; the operational cause was that she did not disengage. She fought to the end of her ability, at her largest size, against an opponent of a different order. Read in the file's terms, this is not a tactical error. It is the logical terminus of every behavior described in this chronology — the project of *not being too small to save him* concluded at maximum size, beside his body, having tried.

She died at approximately sixteen years of published age. Every formation interval described in this section was compressed into roughly six years of her life. She built a secret biochemical project, weaponized it, walked into the Avengers' headquarters, fought a civil war, conducted a love affair with a synthetic boy, hunted a reality-warping former teammate of her father's, accepted a deal with one of the most dangerous men in the world to bring her father back, watched the deal collapse exactly as she had been warned it would, and died next to him. This is the formed identity. Everything subsequent to her resurrection is the question of what happens when that identity is given a second life it did not ask for and did not, in any developmental sense, finish.

---

*End Chronology II. The forging interval is closed. The next section must address what was returned, in what condition, and to what household — and whether the Cassie who walked out of Pleasant Hill is continuous with the Cassie who walked into Doom's throne room, or whether the file is now tracking, in some meaningful sense, two related but distinct subjects under the same name.*


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

## SUBJECT: CASSANDRA ELEANOR LANG
### Coverage: from the formation of the Young Avengers (post-*Disassembled*, approx. subject-age 12) through the conclusion of the Children's Crusade and the immediate post-mortem interval (subject-age ~16, chronologically; functionally indeterminate after restoration).

This is the period in which the subject ceases to be a witnessed child and becomes a self-authored operator. Everything she will be later is laid down here. The file devotes its bulk to this stretch because, longitudinally, this is where the patterns ossify into character.

---

## 1. THE INTERSTITIAL YEAR (Post-*Disassembled*, Pre-*Young Avengers* #1)

The published record is thin for the twelve to eighteen months following Scott Lang's death at Avengers Mansion, and that thinness is itself the evidence. The subject does not appear in panel during this period; she is *absent from her own life*. Reconstructing from later disclosures and behavior in *Young Avengers* #6 and the Heinberg back-matter:

- **Domicile:** primary residence with Margaret Lang and Officer Jim Paxton in the Paxton family home (greater New York metropolitan area, exact municipality varied by writer; treat as suburban Westchester-adjacent). The subject's bedroom is on a second floor; later combat panels show her dropping from a window of remembered height, suggesting practiced egress.
- **School attendance:** middle school, ordinary public, name never specified. Grades not depicted as suffering; subject is competent at compartmentalization from the earliest record.
- **Funeral / interment:** Scott Lang's body was destroyed in the Kree warship sequence. There is no grave. This absence is operationally critical — the subject has nowhere to go to be sad. She will *manufacture* a site of mourning by becoming one.
- **First Pym ingestion (inferred):** sometime in this interstitial year. The mechanism is never panel-shown but is later disclosed as theft from Scott's stockpile (held either at the Paxton residence in a footlocker of his effects, or accessed via a storage unit Scott had maintained — both readings appear across writers). The subject begins covertly self-dosing in a developmental window when her endocrine system is in flux. Behavioral micro-pattern that begins here and persists for the rest of her recorded life: she swallows the particles dry, no water, head tipped back — a tic visible in *Young Avengers* #4 and again, identically, in *Children's Crusade* #5. The gesture is consistent.
- **Concealment behaviors first appearing in this year:** lying about whereabouts, late-night absences explained as "studying at a friend's," the use of bathroom mirror time as a private laboratory for size attempts (she practices in spaces with locks). Maggie does not catch on. Paxton, as a working officer, almost certainly noticed indicators (sleep deficit, mood cycling, unaccounted hours) but did not escalate within the marriage. This is the first documented instance of the subject successfully running a covert life under direct adult surveillance, a competence she will refine for years.

## 2. RECRUITMENT AND THE YOUNG AVENGERS — FIRST OPERATIONAL PERIOD

### 2.1. Entry, *Young Avengers* #4–6

The subject self-presents to the team. This is the defining choice of her adolescence and the founding act of her operational life. Note the texture:

- She does not wait to be invited. She *appears*. She locates the team — through public news footage and through a private archive of her father's Avengers contacts that she has either inherited or stolen — and inserts herself into their headquarters by force of presence rather than credential.
- The cohort she enters: Patriot (Eli Bradley), Hulkling (Teddy Altman), Wiccan (Billy Kaplan), Iron Lad (Nathaniel Richards), and Hawkeye (Kate Bishop). Within weeks she will form the four most durable relationships of her life within this group. They become — and the file uses this phrase precisely — the **functional kin network** that displaces her biological family without ever requiring her to formally renounce it.
- **Confrontation with Jessica Jones and Captain America (*Young Avengers* #5–6):** the subject, having grown to roughly fourteen feet, refuses an order from Steve Rogers to stand down. The specific dialogue varies by reading, but the operational fact is: at age twelve to thirteen, having known of Captain America her entire life as the most authoritative voice her father ever obeyed, she defies him in person and gets away with it. This event recalibrates her ceiling for whom she will accept correction from. The answer, from this point forward, is *almost no one*.
- **Maggie's response to discovery:** when the subject's powers and team membership become known to her mother, Maggie tries to ground her, then tries to involve Jessica Jones as a paid minder. Both attempts fail. The subject treats containment by her mother as a logistical problem to be routed around, not a moral claim. This is the period in which the maternal bond is downgraded, possibly permanently, from authority to affection.

### 2.2. Codename, costume, presentation

- **Codename "Stature"** is self-selected. The lexical choice is diagnostic: not "Giantess," not "Ant-Girl," not a derivative of her father's brand. *Stature* is an abstract noun meaning *the height a person has attained, literal or figurative*. She named herself the answer to her own wound.
- **Original costume:** retains red and black palette with silver accents — Scott's color logic, slightly modified. Helmet design echoes Ant-Man silhouette but with a faceplate that retracts more readily; observed pattern across panels — she retracts the faceplate the moment threat passes, exposing her face. She wants to be seen. The mask is functional, not psychological.
- **Body language at size:** when giant, the subject crouches more than she stands. She does not loom. She kneels to address grounded teammates rather than forcing them to look up. This is a stable mannerism across every writer who draws her at scale, and it is its own data — she enlarges to be effective, not to dominate.

### 2.3. The Vision / Jonas relationship

This relationship is itself a chronological event of the period and deserves a discrete entry.

- **Inception (*Young Avengers* #12):** following the dissolution of Iron Lad back into the Kang timeline, the abandoned Iron Lad armor is fused with operating-system fragments of the original Vision. The resulting entity — Jonas — possesses the memories of the boy the subject was beginning to love and the protocols of an Avenger long dead.
- **Pattern recognition (file):** the subject's first romantic attachment is, in literal terms, a dead Avenger reanimated inside the chassis of a boy who left. Every element of her foundational loss is replicated. She did not select this consciously; the selection function is operating beneath awareness.
- **Behavioral micro-patterns in the relationship:** observed instances of the subject reaching to touch Jonas's chest plate over the spot where a sternum would be — a placement consistent with seeking a heartbeat that does not exist. She holds his hand in public; she sits closer than necessary; she narrates plans to him even when he has not asked. She is *practicing being known*, on a partner who cannot abandon her by dying because he is already, in a meaningful sense, dead.
- **Duration:** this relationship persists, with interruptions, through the entire middle period and ends only with Jonas's destruction by Doctor Doom in *Children's Crusade*. He is the second beloved Avenger she will watch die.

## 3. *CIVIL WAR* AND THE SECRET CABAL PERIOD

### 3.1. Alignment

The subject sides with Captain America's anti-registration faction. The file notes:

- This is the *same Captain America she defied two years earlier*. The alignment is not a submission; it is a coalition of convenience grounded in shared moral instinct. The subject has already established she will not be ordered; she will, however, be *joined*.
- Her stated reasoning, where dialogue is available, centers on her father — Scott Lang would not have registered; Scott Lang spent his adult life as a man the government had a file on. She is, in choosing a side, asking *what would he have done*. This is the longitudinal pattern: in moments of forced moral choice she runs the calculation through the dead father first.

### 3.2. Operational tempo, *Civil War* through *The Initiative*

- Combat exposure increases dramatically. The subject participates in the assault on the Negative Zone prison (Project 42), the final Manhattan engagement, and multiple smaller skirmishes. She is fourteen at most.
- **Injury pattern in this stretch:** crush trauma to the left side (a giant taking a building strike on the off-hand side is the most common loadout), repeat concussive events, and what is implied to be a hairline fracture of the left clavicle in the post-*Civil War* hiatus. None of this is treated by conventional medicine; the team has access to Kree-tech infirmary equipment via Hulkling's lineage and to Wiccan's healing, both of which the subject relies on rather than going to a hospital that would document her.
- **Cardiac stress, unmonitored:** the subject is now changing size in extended combat sequences with no clinical supervision. Her repaired congenital lesion has never been re-evaluated since childhood. The file flags this throughout the period as the single most underappreciated medical risk in her file.

### 3.3. Going underground

In the post-*Civil War* fugitive interval, the subject lives intermittently outside her mother's home. Safehouses include the Bishop family penthouse (Kate Bishop's father's residence, used without his knowledge), the Avengers' Bay Area mansion during the New Avengers' nomadic phase, and at least one extended stay in the Kaplan family home in Long Island, where Rebecca and Jeff Kaplan absorbed the subject as a near-second-daughter without ever demanding a full accounting. The Kaplan household is, longitudinally, the *safest* adult-supervised environment of the subject's adolescence — quieter than her own home, less surveilled than her stepfather's, warm in a way she does not encounter elsewhere.

## 4. *SECRET INVASION* — THE FALSE RETURN

### 4.1. The event

A figure presenting as Scott Lang returns. The subject — and the public — believe, briefly, that he is alive. He is revealed to be a Skrull infiltrator (Criti Noll / one of the Skrull operatives embedded in the long-game) and is killed in the resolution of the invasion.

### 4.2. Effect on the subject

The file emphasizes the under-appreciated severity of this event in the subject's longitudinal record. The episode is:

- The first time she experiences resurrection-fantasy fulfillment. The world hands her her father. She allows herself to believe it. The belief is shown in panel — embraces, conversation, the loosening of the held-shoulders posture that has characterized her since *Disassembled*.
- Then it is revoked, and the revocation is *not* a death but a *revelation of falsity*. Worse than death: the man she hugged was never him. The grief is now contaminated by epistemic doubt. She will, for the rest of the recorded period, second-guess any future return.
- This is the *operationally significant* precursor to her behavior in *Children's Crusade*. When Scott is offered to her again by Doom-via-Wanda, the subject's choice to accept the offer despite obvious indicators of trap is not naïveté; it is a person who has been burned once *deciding to be burned again because the alternative is to refuse what she most wants*. The Skrull episode established the price. She decided to pay it next time.

## 5. THE DARK REIGN / SIEGE INTERVAL

### 5.1. Mighty Avengers tenure

The subject and Vision/Jonas join Hank Pym's iteration of the Mighty Avengers during Norman Osborn's regime. The operational details:

- **Hank Pym as proxy father:** the file notes this carefully. Pym is the *inventor* of the particles her father stole, the man whose science is in her bloodstream. By working under him, the subject is in proximity to the originating substance of her own body. She does not call him by his first name; she calls him "Doctor Pym" with formality unusual for her. The relationship is correct rather than warm. He, in turn, is unsteady around her — she is the unintended downstream consequence of his work, a child made partly out of his physics.
- **Working alongside Jocasta, U.S. Agent, Stature, Vision, and Hercules:** the subject is, in this team, the youngest by decades. She holds her own. Her tactical reads are noted by Pym in dialogue as "frighteningly good for her age," which is the file's preferred kind of evidence — a genius-tier observer of size-physics confirming, in panel, that the subject has internalized the geometry.

### 5.2. *Siege* and the Asgard assault

The subject participates in the final assault on Asgard. She is approximately fifteen. She fights Norman Osborn's Avengers and Sentry's remnants. There is no individuated arc for her in *Siege*; she is one of many. The file notes this only because the *absence* of personal focus is itself a marker — she has reached the point in her career where she can be deployed as a standard unit. The wildness of her *Young Avengers* introduction is now occupational competence.

## 6. *CHILDREN'S CRUSADE* — THE TERMINAL ARC OF THE MIDDLE PERIOD

This is the defining arc of the subject's life prior to her first death. Every prior tendency cited in the foundational workup converges here. The file treats it as a single operational episode lasting roughly nine months of in-universe time.

### 6.1. Initiating decision

The Young Avengers, led by Wiccan's discovery that he may be a son of the Scarlet Witch, set out to locate Wanda. The subject's stated reason for joining: solidarity with Billy. The *underlying* reason, visible in her affect, her insomnia in the team quarters, her tendency to be the first to volunteer for forward reconnaissance: she is going to meet the woman who killed her father. She is going to look her in the face and decide what to do.

### 6.2. The Doom arrangement

When Wanda is located in Latveria, amnesiac, betrothed to Doctor Doom, and when Doom — wielding the chaos magic now bound to him — offers to return the team's dead, the subject is the first to accept. She does not deliberate visibly. The acceptance is, in operational terms, *instantaneous*. The file reads this as the Skrull-precedent in action: she has already decided, years earlier, that the next time she is offered her father she will take the offer.

### 6.3. Scott Lang's second life

For an interval of approximately ten days of in-universe time, Scott Lang is alive in his daughter's vicinity. The micro-patterns observed during this interval are the most psychologically dense passages in the subject's entire record:

- She does not leave his side except when ordered to operational duty. She brings him food. She narrates years he missed — Vision/Jonas, the team, the wars, her own size-changing, the school years he was not at. She does this rapidly, as if she knows.
- She does not tell him about the Pym dosing. This is the only major omission. The file infers shame, specifically the shame of having appropriated his work without permission, but possibly also the protective instinct of not wanting his second life burdened by knowledge of her self-endangerment.
- Physical contact frequency is extreme. She is observed touching his arm, his shoulder, holding his hand, standing within arm's length almost continuously. Her hypervigilance, suppressed for years, now expresses as *over-proximity*. She is keeping him in tactile range so that if the world comes for him she will be the first to know.
- She does not sleep, or sleeps in the same room. The file infers near-total sleep deprivation for the interval.

### 6.4. Loss event, second iteration

In the Latverian throne-room sequence, Doctor Doom — in the engagement that destabilizes the chaos magic — kills Scott Lang. The subject witnesses it directly, at close range, in a body that is the same size as her father's. She is not giant at the moment of his death. She is small, beside him, watching.

She then attacks Doom and is killed by him, immediately, in the same engagement.

### 6.5. Death — the file entry

Cause: catastrophic force trauma inflicted by Doctor Doom. Time-of-death: contemporaneous with Scott Lang's second death. Bodily disposition: recovered, returned to United States, interred in a marked grave (unlike her father). She is, in death, given the funeral her father never had — and she is buried, the file notes, *as her father's daughter*, in a service attended by the Young Avengers, by Maggie and Paxton, and by a small Avengers delegation. Cap is reported present. Jonas is not — he was destroyed earlier in the same arc.

### 6.6. The pattern, named

The arc completes a structure visible across the entire middle period:

1. She lost her father once, at a distance, by surprise (Disassembled).
2. She was offered him falsely (Secret Invasion). She accepted, was wounded by the revocation, and decided that next time she would accept anyway.
3. She was offered him truly, by a worse hand (Children's Crusade). She accepted. She had him. She lost him again, this time at close range, with full witnessing.
4. She died beside him.

The middle period is, in retrospect, a fifteen-year construction of the conditions necessary for her to die in the act of finally being present at her father's death — the death she missed the first time because she was watching it on television. The reparative fantasy was never reunion. It was *being there*. She got there. It killed her.

## 7. THE POSTHUMOUS INTERVAL

Between her death in *Children's Crusade* and her restoration in the post-*Standoff* / *Astonishing Ant-Man* period, the subject does not appear. The file notes the period because:

- **Scott Lang is restored to life first.** He returns. He learns his daughter died trying to bring him back. He carries this for the duration of his solo runs in the interval, and it informs his subsequent willingness to take risks on her behalf that he had previously refused. The dynamic between father and daughter, when she is eventually restored, is inverted from the original — *he* is now the one who has buried *her*, and the asymmetry will color every interaction in the late period.
- **The Young Avengers re-form without her.** The Gillen / McKelvie iteration goes forward in her absence. The team carries her loss as a structural fact. When she returns, she returns to a kin-network that has grieved her and reorganized itself around her absence — the same operation she once performed for her father, now performed for her.

The middle period therefore closes not with her death but with the establishment of the conditions under which her return will be *meaningful*. She had spent her adolescence trying to bring her father back; she will spend her late period being the one brought back. The subject who emerges from restoration in the next chronological section is the same person — the patterns are intact, the wounds are intact, the wiring is intact — but the position in the structure has rotated. She is no longer the daughter waiting. She is the returned one. Whether she knows how to be that, the file does not yet know.

*— End Chronology III. Continue to Chronology IV: Restoration and the Late Period.*


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

## SUBJECT: CASSANDRA ELEANOR "CASSIE" LANG
### File Section: Recent History & Present Disposition

---

## 4.1 — The Re-Entry Period: After Resurrection, Before Reclamation

The most useful place to begin the recent record is the moment Subject re-entered continuity as a living person and a chronologically displaced one.
