The diagnostic value is high — under offer of reunion with a lost loved one, her reality testing degrades predictably. Any operative aware of this can construct nearly irresistible traps. (See Section VIII, Pressure Points.)

### VII.2.d — Idealization

Of Scott (primary), of Cap (secondary), of the Young Avengers cohort (tertiary). The idealization of Scott is the most resistant to evidence — even after she has integrated his criminality and his repeated failures, the affective halo remains. Notable: she does *not* idealize her own competence. The idealization is reserved for others; on herself she is realistic, even self-deprecating. This asymmetry is protective — it prevents the grandiosity that her power-set would otherwise invite.

### VII.2.e — Displacement (Onto Size)

Affect she cannot express in language gets expressed in scale. Anger at being dismissed becomes growth. Fear of being seen becomes shrinkage. Tenderness toward smaller teammates is sometimes expressed by *matching their scale* — she will shrink to peer-level with a smaller hero rather than tower in conversation, a gesture more articulate than any verbal accommodation she makes.

### VII.2.f — Intellectualization (Mild)

Present in her strategic discussions of size-physics — she has learned the geometry, the inverse-square implications, the mass-conservation problems. In moments of high affect she will sometimes pivot to technical discussion of her own power-set, which functions as a small holding device against overwhelm. Less prominent than in her father, who is a heavier intellectualizer.

---

## VII.3 — Mature Stratum (Adaptive, Sublimatory)

### VII.3.a — Sublimation Into Heroic Vocation

The single largest **positive** compensation. The grief, the rage at Wanda, the helplessness of the cardiac child, the longing for the lost father — all of it has been channeled into a vocation that produces real social good. This is sublimation in the precise Freudian sense: the drive is not denied or distorted but *re-aimed* at a culturally valued object. The vocation gives her affect a daily container, gives her body a daily use, and gives her grief a daily meaning. Without it she would almost certainly decompensate; with it she is functional and often joyful.

### VII.3.b — Altruism (Anna Freud's mature defense)

She derives genuine pleasure from rescue and protection — not the manic relief of reparative fantasy, but the sustained satisfaction of having been useful to someone smaller or more frightened than herself. Observable in quieter scenes across the Astonishing Ant-Man and recent Young Avengers material: she lingers with rescued civilians, learns names, follows up. This is mature altruism, not codependent caretaking — she does not need to be needed; she takes pleasure in having helped.

### VII.3.c — Humor

Reliably present. Her humor is warm, self-deprecating, and oriented toward defusing tension in others. She uses size jokes about herself preemptively, which is a sophisticated maneuver — by owning the absurdity of her power before anyone else can mock it, she controls the frame. The humor is rarely cruel and almost never directed at vulnerability in others. This is mature defense in the Vaillant sense and is one of the strongest predictors of her long-term prognosis.

### VII.3.d — Affiliation

Her capacity to build and maintain the Young Avengers as a kinship structure across years, deaths, breaks, and reformations is a mature affiliative defense. She does not merely *belong* to the team; she **maintains** it — checking in on members during quiet periods, holding institutional memory, brokering reconciliations. The team functions as her metabolizing organ for affect she cannot process alone.

### VII.3.e — Anticipation

Less prominent but emergent in the Ewing-era material. She has begun to anticipate father-themed triggers and pre-arrange support before encountering them. This is a developmental gain — earlier in the record she walked into ambushes; in recent material she walks in with a teammate beside her.

### VII.3.f — Forgiveness as Defense

A category that does not appear in standard defensive nosologies but is operationally real in her case. Her refusal to kill Wanda, despite having means, motive, and opportunity, is not weakness or naivete — it is an active defensive choice to *not become the thing that took her father*. By refusing the retributive path she protects her own self-concept as a restorative agent. The defense costs her something each time she exercises it (she does not pretend to have stopped hating Wanda), but the cost is paid in service of a coherent self.

---

## VII.4 — Negative Compensations (Costs and Risks)

### VII.4.a — Chronic Self-Endangerment via Pym Dosing

The compound that gave her her father back to her, biochemically, is also slowly taxing a repaired cardiac substrate. She has never voluntarily reduced usage; she has never sought endocrinological follow-up; she has never had a frank conversation with a cardiologist about size-change loads. This is a long-arc negative compensation — the cost is deferred but real.

### VII.4.b — Identity Fusion with Father

Every gain in autonomy across her career has been hard-won precisely because the original identity-structure was fused with Scott's. Even her independent heroic name (Stinger) is a derivative of his (Ant-Man). She has not yet constructed an alias, a costume aesthetic, or a vocation that is *not legible as Scott-adjacent*. The negative compensation is that her individuation project is perpetually deferred.

### VII.4.c — Re-enactment in Partner Choice

Her romantic gravitation toward the synthetic, the resurrected, the wounded-and-returned is not coincidental. She is staging the loss-and-recovery structure in her love life, presumably in an attempt to master it. The cost: her partners are unusually likely to die, vanish, fracture, or be revealed as not-quite-themselves — meaning the very dynamic she is trying to master keeps re-traumatizing her.

### VII.4.d — Conflict-Avoidance with Maggie

The mother who stayed is consistently under-valued in Cassie's emotional economy. This is a negative compensation in the sense that Cassie *preserves* the idealized father by *demoting* the realistic mother — a stable but distorting solution. The cost is borne by Maggie and, secondarily, by Cassie's own capacity for ordinary, non-mythologized attachment.

### VII.4.e — Self-Erasure in Group Cohesion

Her tendency to position herself as the holder of the Young Avengers means her own needs surface late and quietly. She does not abandon herself outright, but she defers. The pattern is sustainable now; under sufficient stress it would predict burnout, somatic complaint, or sudden withdrawal.

---

## VII.5 — Defense Hierarchy Under Acute Stress

A predictive sequence, derived from observed escalation patterns:

1. **Humor** (first 1–3 seconds of an unexpected stressor): she will try to defuse with a quip, often size-related.
2. **Intellectualization / size-physics talk** (3–15 seconds): she narrates the tactical geometry.
3. **Displacement onto scale** (15–60 seconds): she begins micro-adjusting size as affect rises.
4. **Reaction formation** (1–5 minutes): she commits to a protective or rescue posture, often growth-scaled.
5. **Counterphobic charge** (5+ minutes, or immediately if a loved one is in danger): she moves toward the threat regardless of tactical sense.
6. **Reparative fantasy override** (engaged only when reunion-with-dead material is in play): reality testing degrades, self-preservation deprioritizes, sacrificial behavior becomes likely.

Step 6 is where she died once. It is the step to watch for.

---

## VII.6 — Prognostic Note

The defensive architecture is *front-loaded with mature mechanisms* (sublimation, altruism, humor, affiliation, forgiveness) sitting atop a primitive substrate that has never been fully metabolized (somatic identification with the lost father, reparative fantasy under reunion-offers). This is an unusual and largely favorable structure: she functions well, loves well, works well, and the deep wound remains specific and locatable rather than diffuse. With sustained relational work — and, critically, with a period of life in which her father remains *steadily alive and steadily ordinary* long enough for her to grieve the version she lost — the primitive substrate is metabolizable. Without that, the architecture holds, but the cost continues to accrue in the body she has been quietly taxing since adolescence.

*— End Section VII. Continue to Section VIII: Pressure Points & Exploitable Vectors.*


## Longitudinal Psychological Profile

# Longitudinal Psychological Profile — Cassandra Eleanor Lang

*Decade-by-decade clinical mapping. Frameworks invoked: Bowlby/Ainsworth attachment; Mahler separation-individuation; Eriksonian stage theory; Fenichel on counterphobia; Klein on identification with the lost object; Stroebe & Schut's dual-process model of bereavement; Linehan's biosocial model of emotion regulation; Marcia's identity-status paradigm; Herman on complex traumatic bereavement. Derivations are from the published behavioral record; nothing endogenous is assumed.*

---

## I. The Pre-Powered Decade (c. ages 0–10; Marvel Premiere #47 through the late Scott Lang solo and Avengers tenure)

### Affect regulation

The earliest depicted Cassie is, technically, a sick child. Her published infancy is dominated by a cardiac defect so severe that her continued life is contingent on an outside surgeon being delivered to her by means of a felony. This is the formative affective fact: **the body is unreliable, but love is operationalized as rescue**. The infant/toddler whose chest does not work correctly is held by adults whose faces are alternately tender and terrified — Maggie's exhausted vigilance, Scott's panic-driven competence. Cassie's nervous system is calibrated in this period to register adult dysregulation as the *ambient weather* of love. She learns very early that being loved means being the cause of someone's fear.

By the time she is ambulatory and verbal (the late 1980s domestic scenes), she presents as a precociously regulated child — bright affect, easy laughter with Scott, an evident capacity to self-soothe through stuffed animals and routinized play with her father's gear. The pattern that will define her later years is already visible in miniature: when separated from Scott (his prison stints, his Fantastic Four tours, his Avengers reserve work), she does not collapse into protest; she *waits*, often at a window, often with a kept object of his. This is the early signature of an **anxious-preoccupied attachment with strong organizing capacity** — she does not become disorganized in his absence; she structures herself around it.

### Cognitive style

In the pre-powered years she is depicted as a precocious narrator of her own life — quick with questions, fast to remember details about Pym particles, ants, and her father's working life. Her cognition is **concrete-operational with a magical-realist overlay** appropriate to her actual world (she has met the Fantastic Four; reality is permeable). She is not credulous; she is correctly calibrated for her environment, which happens to include shrinking men. Already her thinking is **person-centered rather than abstract** — she remembers what people said, what they wore, what they did, in granular detail. This is the substrate of what will later become her gift for holding a team together.

### Self-concept

Functionally a **mirror-self organized around the father**. Asked who she is, the early Cassie answers, in effect, *Scott Lang's daughter, who almost died once*. Erikson's industry-vs-inferiority stage (roughly ages 6–10) is depicted as productively resolved on the surface — she is competent at school, at play, at the social tasks of childhood — but the substrate is fragile because her sense of *mattering* is keyed to a single relationship. She does not yet know she is a person separable from being loved by Scott. This is normal for the age; it is the *failure to revise it later* that becomes diagnostic.

---

## II. The Foundational Rupture and Latency Aftermath (c. ages 10–13; Avengers Disassembled through pre–Young Avengers)

### Affect regulation

The televised immolation of Scott Lang at Avengers Mansion is the central event of Cassie's interior life. Note the specific traumatogenic geometry: **mediated witnessing** (she sees it on a screen), **no body**, **no warning**, **a perpetrator who is morally categorized as one of the good ones**. Each of these is independently a complicating factor in bereavement; together they produce what Herman would classify as a **complex traumatic bereavement** with strong **disenfranchised-grief** features (society mourns the Avenger; Cassie mourns the father, and the two grievances are not commensurable).

Her affect regulation in this period — derivable from her behavior in the run-up to Young Avengers #1 — shifts from the organized waiting of the pre-powered years to a **suppressed, internalized hypervigilance**. She does not act out at home in a conduct-disorder sense. Instead she becomes *quiet in a particular way* — the quiet of a child who has decided that grown-ups cannot help and has begun to plan. The covert Pym-dosing begins in this window. Read clinically, this is a **somatic-behavioral grief expression** — a child unable to metabolize affect through language metabolizing it through *taking the father's substance into her body*. Klein's identification with the lost object is, in Cassie's case, almost literal: she is ingesting him.

Affect itself in this period is **constricted in expression, intense in interior**. She does not cry in front of Jim Paxton. She does cry, one infers, in private, with the kept gear. The dual-process model of bereavement (Stroebe & Schut) describes healthy mourning as oscillation between loss-orientation and restoration-orientation; Cassie at this age has **collapsed both poles into a single project — restoration AS loss-orientation**. She will restore her father by becoming him. There is no oscillation; there is only the plan.

### Cognitive style

Sharpening, with a notable **goal-binding** quality. She acquires extensive operational knowledge — Pym-particle dosing, suit mechanics, Avengers personnel, surveillance of her father's old contacts — and does so silently. This is not the cognition of a depressed child; it is the cognition of a child who has found a problem she can solve. **Means-end reasoning becomes hypertrophied**; abstract self-reflection is deferred. She does not ask *why* she wants to become a hero; she works out *how*.

A subtle but important feature: she begins to develop the **moral-categorical thinking** that will mark her Young Avengers years. The Avengers establishment that failed her father is filed as suspect; the *idea* of the Avengers that her father loved is preserved as sacred. This split — institution-bad / ideal-good — is a cognitive defense that lets her continue to want to be what killed him.

### Self-concept

This is the period in which **identification with the lost object** crystallizes into a project. The pre-rupture self-concept ("Scott Lang's daughter, who almost died") becomes the post-rupture self-concept ("Scott Lang's *continuation*, who must not let him fully die"). Marcia's identity-status framework is useful here: most children this age are in **identity diffusion**; Cassie has skipped diffusion and gone directly into **identity foreclosure** — adopting an identity wholesale from a parental template without the intervening exploratory crisis. Foreclosure is stable and functional; it is also brittle in characteristic ways, which will surface in her late teens.

---

## III. The Young Avengers Decade (c. ages 13–16 narrative-time, 2005–2012 publication-time; Young Avengers through Children's Crusade)

### Affect regulation

This is Cassie's first period of **integrated, peer-mediated affect regulation**. The Young Avengers function, in Linehan's biosocial terms, as the **validating environment** her domestic life cannot provide — a group of similarly-bereaved or similarly-displaced adolescents whose emotional language matches hers. With Kate Bishop, Eli Bradley, Teddy Altman, Billy Kaplan, Tommy Shepherd, and the Vision-Jonas, she becomes capable of **expressed affect in real time** — humor, anger, grief, tenderness — to a degree she does not display at home.

Two regulatory patterns become observable and persistent:

1. **Size-as-affect-regulation.** When overwhelmed, threatened, or unheard, Cassie grows. The behavior is not purely tactical; the growth precedes the tactical need in many incidents. Physiologically, the act of size-change appears to function for her the way a deep inhalation or a postural expansion functions for the non-powered — a **somatic discharge of sympathetic arousal** that also makes her literally impossible to dismiss. This is the cleanest available example in the published record of a power that is also a coping mechanism.

2. **Action-as-rumination-displacement.** Cassie does not stew. When she is upset, she moves — patrols, trains, finds a mission. This is adaptive in the short term and **anti-mentalizing in the long term**; it prevents the reflective processing that grief eventually requires. Across the Young Avengers years she is consistently the team member who proposes the next operation when the group is paralyzed by feeling.

Dysregulation is **stimulus-specific and predictable**: any cue invoking Scott (his name, his enemies, his old colleagues, the prospect of his return) produces a measurable narrowing of attention, escalation of voice, and an increased probability of size-up. Outside that stimulus class she is one of the more regulated members of the team.

### Cognitive style

Verbally fluent, morally engaged, **strategically capable under specific conditions**. Size-changing combat requires fast geometric and inertial reasoning — Cassie demonstrates this competence repeatedly — and her tactical thinking improves markedly across the Young Avengers run as she internalizes peer feedback (notably from Kate Bishop, whose dryness counter-regulates Cassie's earnestness).

The dominant cognitive defense in this period is **moral splitting**, in the early Kleinian sense — clean categorizations of authority figures into trustworthy and untrustworthy, with Captain America in the former column and the Stark/registration apparatus in the latter. This is age-appropriate and politically substantive (her position on registration is principled), but it also serves the deeper function of **preserving an internal good-Avengers object** that her father can have died for without his death being absurd. To let the categories blur would be to admit that Scott died for a flawed institution; that admission is psychologically unaffordable until later.

A second cognitive feature emerges in this period: **a reparative rather than retributive moral frame**. Given the Scarlet Witch as the proximate cause of her father's death, Cassie's organizing question across Children's Crusade is not *how do I punish her* but *how do I undo what she did*. This is a strikingly mature cognitive orientation in an adolescent and is the single best evidence that her core character is restorative — but it is also the cognition that walks her into Doom's trap. **Reparative cognition is exploitable cognition** when the world contains entities willing to offer fraudulent repairs.

### Self-concept

The foreclosed identity of the prior period becomes **publicly performed and externally validated** in the Young Avengers years. She is *Stature*. She is on the team. She is in the costume. The identity holds because the social environment confirms it.

Crucially, however, the romantic and affiliative choices of this period reveal what the surface identity is built on. She partners with the Vision-Jonas — a synthetic being constructed from the memory-template of a dead Avenger, who himself contains the longing of a returned-and-lost figure. She seeks out her father's old colleagues. She gravitates to the bereaved (Billy and Tommy, Eli's complicated lineage). The **affiliative pattern is uniformly organized around loss and return**. Self-concept at this point is best described as **a competent surface built over an unmetabolized core**.

The Children's Crusade arc is the moment the core breaks the surface. Offered her father back, she takes him; offered his second death, she dies trying to prevent it. In Eriksonian terms she has skipped past *identity-vs-role-confusion* and *intimacy-vs-isolation* and gone straight to a kind of premature **generativity-vs-stagnation** in service of a parent — and the cost of that compression is her life.

---

## IV. The Disjunctive Resurrection and Junior-Operator Period (c. ages 12–15 post-resurrection; Avengers: Standoff / Astonishing Ant-Man / Ant-Man and the Wasp)

### Affect regulation

Cassie returns from death in a body chronologically younger than the self that died. This is a **somatic-temporal disjunction** with no clean clinical analogue — the nearest comparison is the dissociative experience of long-coma survivors who wake with an altered body, but compounded by a literal age-rollback. Her affective baseline in this period is **subtly off-register**: writers depict a child whose emotional vocabulary outruns her physiology, who reacts to certain stimuli with adolescent affect from a pre-adolescent body, and whose father (now alive again, and now the parent of a daughter who has died and returned) handles her with a tentativeness that is its own dysregulating cue.

Regulation in this period is **provisional and externally scaffolded**. She borrows stability from Scott's renewed presence and from the structured environment of his security company. She is more affect-expressive than in the latency aftermath but less integrated than at her Young Avengers peak. The Darren Cross hostage incident reactivates the *foundational scene of her existence* (Cross was the villain of her father's origin, the proximate cause of the original Pym-particle theft) and Cassie's response is striking for its **lack of fresh trauma** — she has, in effect, already metabolized Cross at the level of family myth. The pattern here is a **mature defense by narrative integration**: the recurring antagonist becomes a known quantity, and known quantities are less destabilizing than novel ones.

### Cognitive style

Cognitive functioning in this period is **observably more flexible** than in the Young Avengers run. The reparative-vs-retributive frame remains, but the splitting softens. She can now hold Scott as *flawed and lovable* in the same thought — a categorical integration that her younger self could not perform. This is the first clear evidence of movement from foreclosed identity toward **identity-moratorium** in Marcia's sense: she is finally beginning to *explore* who she might be rather than executing a predetermined plan.

The Stinger identity is diagnostic in this regard. **Stature** was a name chosen to mean *I am as big as he is*. **Stinger** is a name with no patrilineal weight — a new operational identity Scott gives her, and which she accepts on her own terms. Small detail, large meaning: the choice to wear a name that is not a translation of her father's is the first observable act of differentiation in her published life.

### Self-concept

Beginning to **decohere productively**. The foreclosed Scott-derived identity no longer fits cleanly over the resurrected body, and Cassie shows, for the first time, signs of mild identity confusion that are actually *healthy* — they are the confusion of someone who has the cognitive room to ask new questions. She experiments with autonomy from Scott (operating independently, making her own moral judgments about his activities), tests new peer configurations, and exhibits the beginnings of a **separable adult self** that does not require the father as scaffolding.

The unfinished work, in this period, is the **integration of the death experience itself**. She does not appear to have a vocabulary for what it was to die, and the books do not give her one. This is, clinically, a buried charge.

---

## V. The Contemporary Period (recent runs; Young Avengers reformations, generational-bridge positioning)

### Affect regulation

The contemporary Cassie shows the most fully **dual-process** affect pattern of her career — capacity to oscillate between loss-orientation (memorializing, mourning, holding her father's history) and restoration-orientation (building a present life, contributing to teams, holding others) without collapsing either into the other. This is the first period in which she can be sad about her father in a *finished* way, rather than in a way that requires immediate corrective action.

Regulation strategies have matured:

- **Size-as-affect-regulation persists**, but is now used more sparingly and with metacognitive awareness — she notices when she is growing because she is angry rather than because she needs to be tall.
- **Peer co-regulation through the reformed Young Avengers** has deepened; she is now often the *regulator* for less-stable teammates rather than the one being regulated.
- **Solitary processing capacity has grown** — she is depicted, in recent material, as someone who can sit with feeling for a sustained period without converting it into action.

Stimulus-specific dysregulation around father-themed and reunion-with-the-dead material remains the single residual vulnerability. It is the cleanest exploitable vector in her psychological profile and is unlikely ever to be fully extinguished — too much of her structure was built on it.

### Cognitive style

The reparative frame is now **integrated with mature risk assessment**. She still wants to repair, but she is increasingly able to recognize the difference between a genuine repair opportunity and a baited one. The Children's Crusade pattern — accepting any offer of reunion — has been at least partially cognitively corrected by experience.

Cognitively she has also developed a **generational lens** — an awareness that she is now older than the Young Avengers cohort she once joined as the youngest, and that there are newer arrivals to whom she occupies the role Kate Bishop or Captain America once occupied for her. This **role-reversal cognition** is one of the most reliable engines of adult identity formation; it is producing real growth in her self-concept.

### Self-concept

She is, for the first time in her published life, **observably in identity-achievement territory** (Marcia) — having explored, having committed, having chosen the heroic vocation not because her father did it but because she has examined it and found it hers. The foreclosed Scott-template self has not been demolished; it has been **incorporated rather than worn**. The third Cassie that the analytical workup hypothesized — the one underneath *daughter* and *Stature* — is becoming visible in outline, though not yet fully drawn.

The grief is not gone. The bereavement is **prolonged-grief-spectrum and will likely remain so** — the cycle of loss-and-return she lived is not the kind of bereavement that resolves; it integrates. What has changed is that grief no longer organizes the entirety of her behavior. It is now one fact among many, rather than the sun around which the rest orbits.

---

## VI. Cross-Sectional Summary of Evolution

| Domain | Pre-rupture | Latency aftermath | Young Avengers | Resurrection / junior op | Contemporary |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| **Dominant affect** | Bright with ambient parental fear | Constricted, hypervigilant | Expressive, peer-mediated, size-discharged | Provisional, externally scaffolded | Oscillating, integrated |
| **Regulation strategy** | Routinized play, kept objects | Covert action-planning, somatic ingestion | Action-displacement, size-as-discharge | Borrowed stability, narrative integration | Metacognitive, co-regulating others |
| **Cognitive defense** | None salient | Goal-binding, moral split begins | Splitting, reparative frame | Splitting softens, reparative-with-caveat | Integrated reparative with risk-weighting |
| **Identity status (Marcia)** | Pre-stage, mirrored | Foreclosure crystallizing | Foreclosure performed and validated | Moratorium opening | Achievement, in early consolidation |
| **Attachment expression** | Anxious-preoccupied, waiting | Identification with lost object | Idealizing fusion with surrogate kin | Tentative differentiation | Differentiated bond, integrated mourning |
| **Primary vulnerability** | Somatic fragility | Disenfranchised grief, isolation | Offers of reunion, threats to Scott | Body-self disjunction | Residual reunion-cue vulnerability |
| **Primary strength** | Capacity to organize around love | Capacity to plan in silence | Capacity to build chosen family | Capacity to integrate recurrence | Capacity to generativity without self-erasure |

---

## VII. Clinical Trajectory in One Sentence

Cassie Lang's psychological history is the **slow conversion of a foreclosed mourning-identity into an achieved heroic self** — a movement from *being the continuation of a dead father* to *being a person who once was a continuation and is now also something else* — accomplished without ever fully resolving the underlying prolonged grief, because that grief is constitutive rather than incidental, and the most adaptive available outcome is not its erasure but its **integration as a stable feature of an otherwise functional adult self**.

That integration is, at present, partial. It is also further along than at any prior point in her published life. The trajectory is favorable. The vulnerabilities are specific, locatable, and — to a knowledgeable adversary — exploitable in precisely one direction: the offer of the dead returned.

*— End longitudinal psychological profile.*


## Psychiatric Assessment & Differential

# PSYCHIATRIC ASSESSMENT & DIFFERENTIAL — SUBJECT: CASSANDRA E. LANG

**Classification:** Longitudinal psychiatric formulation, derived from observable behavioral record across approximately three decades of published canonical material (1979–present), with attention to developmental sequencing, episode-linked symptomatology, and the structural distortions imposed by repeated death/resurrection events on normative grief course.

**Caveat re: framework:** Standard DSM-5-TR and ICD-11 nosology is built on populations who experience one life and one body. The subject has died and returned; has lost adolescent years to chronological reset; has been romantically involved with at least one synthetic/reconstructed person who is, technically, her dead boyfriend's data-ghost. The diagnostic instruments strain at these points. Where they strain, the formulation is annotated accordingly.

---

## I. PRIMARY DIAGNOSIS

### Prolonged Grief Disorder (DSM-5-TR 309.89 / ICD-11 6B42) — CONFIRMED, chronic, with atypical recursion features

This is the load-bearing diagnosis and the one from which most secondary phenomenology flows.

**Criterion-by-criterion fit:**

- **A. Death of a person close to the bereaved at least 12 months ago (6 months for children/adolescents).** Scott Lang's death in the destruction of Avengers Mansion (Disassembled arc) occurred when the subject was approximately 10–12. Bereavement duration is now measured in years, intermittently interrupted by partial returns (Skrull impostor, Doom-resurrected briefly, eventual restoration), each of which has functioned as a re-bereavement rather than a resolution.

- **B. Persistent grief response characterized by yearning/longing for the deceased and/or preoccupation with thoughts or memories of the deceased.** Pervasive and structuring. The subject's entire vocational choice, codename, costume, particle source, peer group, and partner-selection pattern are organized around the lost father. Yearning is not episodic — it is the operating system.

- **C. Symptoms (≥3 of 8) clinically significant most days for ≥1 month:**
  1. *Identity disruption ("a part of me has died"):* Met definitively. Her self-concept is structurally fused with the father's identity — she becomes a smaller version of him biologically.
  2. *Marked sense of disbelief about the death:* Met during the early Young Avengers period; mitigated in later runs.
  3. *Avoidance of reminders the person is dead:* Partially met inversely — she does not avoid reminders; she manufactures them. This is an atypical presentation where the symptom is inverted from avoidance to *compulsive approach*. Clinically equivalent dysfunction.
  4. *Intense emotional pain related to the death:* Met. Reactivated cleanly by every father-themed stimulus.
  5. *Difficulty reintegrating into relationships and activities after the death:* Met. She skipped the developmental tasks of mid-adolescence to pursue hero work. Normative peer-relationships, schooling, sexual/romantic exploration outside the cape community are largely absent from the record.
  6. *Emotional numbness:* Not met. Affective range is preserved.
  7. *Feeling that life is meaningless:* Not met. Meaning is hyper-supplied by the grief project itself.
  8. *Intense loneliness:* Partially met, masked by chosen-family attachments.

- **D. Distress/impairment in functioning.** Met, though heroically rather than catastrophically.

- **E. Bereavement reaction exceeds expected social, cultural, religious norms.** Decisively met — most bereaved children do not chemically transform their bodies to resemble the deceased parent.

**Atypical / case-specific features warranting note:**

The diagnostic literature on PGD presumes a unidirectional bereavement: someone dies, stays dead, and the survivor either resolves or fails to resolve grief over time. The subject's bereavement is **recursive** — the object of grief has been returned to her, taken away again, returned again. This produces a clinical picture not well captured by existing nosology. Each return is, paradoxically, retraumatizing: it confirms that reunion is *possible*, which makes every subsequent loss the foreclosure of a possibility rather than the acceptance of a fact. The standard PGD recovery trajectory — gradual diminution of acute yearning, integration of the loss into a continuing-bond representation — has been *prevented by the universe itself*. She is not a treatment-failure; she is a person whose grief has been actively, externally, repeatedly re-opened.

**Course:** Insidious onset at the time of death, peaking in adolescent Young Avengers period (defiance, runaway behavior, Pym theft, identity reconstitution around father). Acute exacerbation during Children's Crusade (re-enactment to the point of self-fatality). Post-resurrection, gradual partial integration in Astonishing Ant-Man and subsequent runs as she develops a relationship with the *living, flawed* Scott rather than the *lost, idealized* Scott. Current status: subacute, integrated, no longer driving impairment but still structuring identity and choice.

---

## II. CONTRIBUTORY / COMORBID FEATURES

### A. Counterphobic personality organization (Fenichel, 1939; not a DSM category, retained because nothing in DSM captures the pattern as well)

The subject systematically orients toward what should frighten her. Specific instances:

- A child whose body almost killed her in cardiac infancy chooses a power that places extraordinary load on the cardiovascular system.
- A girl whose father was killed doing hero work pursues hero work.
- A bereaved adolescent enters romantic attachment with a reconstructed dead person — a partner whose ontological status (Vision/Jonas) literally instantiates the structure of her primary loss.
- A formerly-dead person, post-resurrection, returns immediately to combat.

This pattern is not impulsive thrill-seeking (which would have a hedonic signature absent here) and not parasuicidal (no death wish; her self-preservation impulses are intact when not in re-enactment). It is the organized inversion of avoidance: by mastering what she fears, she denies the fear power over her. This is psychologically productive in many domains and clinically dangerous in a few — particularly any domain involving the cardiovascular system or reunion offers from supernatural agents.

### B. Pathological identification with the lost object

Distinct from healthy identification (Klein's reparative identification) and from full introjective fusion. Sits somewhere between. She has become biologically what her father was technologically — the Pym particles, originally an exogenous tool he wore, are now endogenous to her endocrine system. This is the *materialization* of identification: not "I am like him" but "his power is in my body." Clinically, this functions as a continuing-bond strategy carried beyond its adaptive range. It has been protective (provided meaning, vocation, community) and limiting (forestalled the development of a self distinct from the lost father).

### C. Re-enactment compulsion (Freud, 1920; modern formulation: trauma-driven repetition)

The Children's Crusade arc is the diagnostic incident. Given the choice between killing the agent of her father's death (which would offer no recovery) and pursuing his resurrection (which would), she chose resurrection. The resurrection succeeded briefly. The father was then killed again, in front of her, by a different agent. She was killed in the same engagement. The structure of the original trauma — *father dies in front of her, by the act of a powerful figure she cannot stop* — was reproduced almost exactly, with the addition that she now joined him in the death. The compulsion to repeat had achieved its terminal form: union with the lost object through shared annihilation. This is the clearest single piece of evidence in the record for an unresolved traumatic-repetition pattern.

### D. Chronic compulsive use of an unregulated bioactive compound

The Pym particle self-dosing meets surface criteria for a substance-related disorder pattern: covert acquisition, concealment from family, escalation, continuation despite known physical risk (cardiac), tolerance development (autonomic integration), and functional impairment in non-hero domains. **It is not, however, classifiable as a Substance Use Disorder** because the reinforcing properties are not hedonic or neurochemical-reward-driven but *symbolic* — the act is grief enactment, not intoxication. The correct framing is **a behavioral/somatic symptom of complicated bereavement**, in which the substance is incidental to the symbolic function. (Analog: a widow who wears her dead husband's clothes daily does not have a clothing disorder.)

That said, the medical risk profile is real and would warrant the same level of monitoring as a chronic exogenous endocrine exposure in a patient with a repaired congenital cardiac defect: serial echocardiography, attention to growth-plate effects pre-resurrection, neurochemical screening for off-target effects of Pym physics on the CNS.

### E. Identity disruption secondary to chronological discontinuity (no DSM analog; novel)

The subject died as an older adolescent and was restored as a younger child, then matured forward again. This produces a state for which no diagnostic category exists: a coherent narrative-self carrying memories of an age the body no longer occupies, then re-living developmental years that were already lived. The closest clinical analog is the disorientation reported by patients recovering from severe, prolonged dissociative fugue, but this undersells the somatic dimension. Subject appears to have integrated this remarkably well, which is itself diagnostically interesting and probably attributable to the strength of her continuing-bond identification with the father, which provides a thread of continuity that survives any bodily disjunction.

---

## III. RIGOROUS DIFFERENTIAL — WHAT SHE IS NOT, AND WHY

The diagnostic temptation with a case this dramatic is to over-label. Discipline requires the opposite. The following diagnoses have been considered and *excluded* on specific evidence.

### A. Major Depressive Disorder — EXCLUDED

Despite extensive and sustained bereavement, the subject does not meet MDD criteria across any depicted period. She exhibits:
- Preserved capacity for pleasure (Young Avengers camaraderie, romantic engagement, humor in combat banter).
- Preserved energy and goal-directedness (in fact, hyperdirected toward the grief project).
- No psychomotor retardation; if anything, agitation in service of goals.
- No depressive cognitive triad (negative views of self, world, future). Self-view is actively grandiose-adjacent; world-view is restorative; future-view is hopeful.
- No vegetative signs apparent on the record.

What looks superficially like depressive symptomatology (preoccupation with the dead, distress at reminders) is grief, not depression. The distinction is clinically meaningful: grief is *for* something, depression is *about* the self.

### B. Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder — EXCLUDED (subthreshold features only)

The Disassembled event meets Criterion A (witnessed traumatic death of close family). The subject does not, however, exhibit the full B/C/D/E cluster:
- *Intrusive symptoms (B):* No depicted flashbacks, intrusive memories outside of contextually appropriate moments, or trauma-themed nightmares.
- *Avoidance (C):* Inverted to compulsive approach (see counterphobic organization above) — not the avoidance the criterion describes.
- *Negative alterations in cognition/mood (D):* Mood is grief-toned but not pervasively negative; cognition is intact.
- *Arousal alterations (E):* No baseline hypervigilance, exaggerated startle, or sleep disturbance reported.

The trauma is *foundational* to character structure rather than *intrusive* into present functioning. This is an important distinction. PTSD is what happens when trauma cannot be integrated; her trauma has been integrated, perhaps too thoroughly — it has become structural rather than symptomatic.

### C. Borderline Personality Disorder — EXCLUDED

Surface features that might suggest BPD (early loss of parent, identity disturbance, risk-taking, intense relationships) dissolve under specific criterion analysis:
- *Abandonment fears:* The relevant abandonments have already occurred — repeatedly — and the subject has not developed the frantic-avoidance pattern. She mourns; she does not panic.
- *Unstable interpersonal relationships:* The opposite. Her attachments are *over-stable*. The Young Avengers cohort has been her primary peer group across virtually her entire post-Disassembled life. Romantic attachments are loyal to the point of pursuing partners across ontological state changes.
- *Identity disturbance:* Present, but coherently organized around a stable nucleus (father-identification). BPD identity disturbance is chaotic and contentless; hers is over-determined and over-full.
- *Impulsivity in ≥2 potentially damaging areas:* Not met. Risk-taking is goal-directed.
- *Suicidal/self-mutilating behavior:* Not met. The Children's Crusade death was re-enactment, not self-harm.
- *Affective instability:* Not met. Baseline is steady; reactivity is stimulus-specific (father-themed material) rather than ambient.
- *Chronic emptiness:* Definitively not met. She is, if anything, chronically *full* — of meaning, mission, attachment, mourning.
- *Inappropriate intense anger:* Not characteristic.
- *Stress-related paranoid ideation/dissociation:* Not present.

Two criteria, partially. Five required. Not BPD.

### D. Narcissistic Personality Disorder — EXCLUDED

The size-change power and the heroic identity could superficially read as grandiosity. Investigation does not support a personality-disorder-level pattern:
- The grandiosity is *situational* (during heroic operations) rather than pervasive.
- Empathy is preserved and prominent — she forgives Wanda Maximoff, which is the single most empathically demanding act in her record.
- She tolerates correction and supervision from peers (Patriot, Wiccan, Hawkeye-Kate Bishop) without narcissistic injury.
- No exploitation pattern in relationships; she is, if anything, over-giving.
- No envy pattern visible.

What looks like grandiosity is *compensatory enlargement* against a wound of helplessness — a specific symptom, not a character structure.

### E. Substance Use Disorder (Pym particle) — EXCLUDED in favor of grief-symptom framing

See II.D above. Surface criteria met, but the reinforcing function is symbolic rather than neurochemical. Pulling the substance without addressing the bereavement would not resolve the behavior; addressing the bereavement makes the substance use clinically tractable.

### F. Conduct Disorder / Antisocial features (adolescent presentation) — EXCLUDED

The Young Avengers period included theft (Pym particles), running away from home, deception of parents, threats toward adults, and resistance to law enforcement (Civil War). Criterion-by-criterion this looks superficially concerning. It is not Conduct Disorder because:
- All depicted antisocial behavior is *principled and goal-bound* (to do hero work, to access the father-identification project).
- No cruelty toward people or animals.
- No deceitfulness for instrumental gain outside of the hero project.
- No theft for material benefit.
- Strong empathic capacity and intact remorse processing.
- Pro-social peer affiliation (the Young Avengers are not a delinquent peer group; they are a vocational guild).

The defiance is closer to *principled adolescent moral commitment in a context of weak parental authority* than to conduct pathology.

### G. Delusional Disorder / Psychotic-spectrum considerations — EXCLUDED

The subject's belief that she can recover her father from death is, in a normal universe, delusional. In her universe, it is empirically correct — Doom did resurrect Scott; the resurrection was real; Scott was returned to her, however briefly. Reality-testing must be evaluated against the actual reality the subject inhabits. By that standard, she is not delusional; she has *evidence-based hope*, however tragic.

Similarly, her communications with synthetic/reconstructed beings (Vision-Jonas) are not hallucinatory; the beings exist and are interacting with her. Psychotic-spectrum diagnoses are inapplicable.

### H. Autism Spectrum / neurodevelopmental considerations — NOT INDICATED

No evidence on the record. Social cognition is excellent; theory of mind is strong (the forgiveness of Wanda requires sophisticated mentalization of another's diminished agency under chaos magic); sensory profile unremarkable. Mentioned and excluded for completeness.

### I. Reactive Attachment / Disinhibited Social Engagement (childhood-onset) — EXCLUDED

Early attachment to both parents was secure and warm despite Scott's intermittent availability. The disturbance emerged from a discrete traumatic loss in middle childhood, not from early caregiving disruption. Wrong etiology, wrong developmental window.

### J. Bipolar-spectrum disorder — EXCLUDED

The escalation pattern under stress (verbal → size-up → combat) can look superficially like manic activation. It is not:
- Episodic only in response to specific stimuli, not cyclical.
- Returns rapidly to baseline once stimulus is removed.
- No expansive mood independent of context.
- No reduced need for sleep, pressured speech, racing thoughts, or hypersexual disinhibition on record.
- No depressive episodes of MDD-level severity (see A).

Mentioned because the *physical* enlargement under stress is a striking image that invites the metaphor of mania. The metaphor does not survive criterion-based examination.

---

## IV. ETIOLOGICAL FORMULATION

The psychiatry here is *consequent on the biography*. Nothing endogenous is required to explain the presentation. The causal sequence:

1. **Pre-morbid substrate:** A child with a body that nearly killed her in infancy and a father whose love for her was demonstrated through criminal self-sacrifice. From the earliest moment of conscious memory, she knew she was loved at cost and survived by intervention. This produces a baseline self-concept in which existence itself is contingent and indebted.

2. **Primary traumatic loss:** The televised death of the father at developmental age 10–12, in a context where the killing agent was a member of the institution he had died trying to belong to. No body, no funeral that could complete the loss, no clear villain to blame (Wanda was herself a casualty of her own breakdown). This is a textbook setup for complicated/prolonged grief.

3. **Defensive consolidation:** Through early adolescence, covert Pym dosing and identity reconstitution around the father provide a workable, if pathological, solution. The grief is converted into a vocation. The vocation is reinforced by chosen-family attachments (Young Avengers) that simultaneously serve developmental needs and grief needs.

4. **Re-enactment to fatality:** Children's Crusade. The defensive structure that had organized grief reaches its terminal expression in the attempt to literally retrieve the lost object, ending in shared death.

5. **Post-resurrection re-formation:** Restoration introduces novel disorganization (chronological reset, somatic disjunction) but also offers the opportunity for grief work that the original timeline had foreclosed. In current material, the subject is doing some of this work — relating to the *flawed living father* rather than the *idealized lost father*. This is the right trajectory.

---

## V. PROGNOSTIC NOTES

**Favorable factors:** Preserved affective range; strong chosen-family support; intact moral compass; demonstrated capacity for forgiveness (the single most prognostically meaningful behavior in her record); growing tolerance for her father's imperfection (which is the prerequisite for resolving the idealization and thus the grief); good cognitive resources; pro-social vocation.

**Unfavorable factors:** Continued exposure to a universe in which death is reversible (forecloses normative grief resolution); chronic Pym exposure with cardiac risk; counterphobic pattern likely to persist and likely to be exploited by sophisticated adversaries; identity remains insufficiently differentiated from father-identification.

**Highest-risk scenarios going forward:** Any future loss of Scott Lang (which the trajectory of the universe makes likely); any offer of reunion-with-the-dead from a malign actor (the single largest exploitable vector in her psychology); any threat to the Young Avengers cohort as a unit; any forced confrontation with the question of whether there is a Cassie underneath Stature/Stinger/daughter-of-Scott.

**Lowest-risk scenarios:** Stable operational tempo with peer team; living father in regular contact; gradual deepening of a non-hero identity (education, peer relationships outside the cape community, romantic partners who are not revenants).

---

## VI. SUMMARY STATEMENT

Cassandra Lang presents as a case of chronic Prolonged Grief Disorder, atypically structured by a recursive bereavement that the standard diagnostic framework was not designed to accommodate, with counterphobic personality organization, pathological identification with the lost object expressed somatically through chronic exogenous bio-compound use, and a re-enactment pattern that has previously been fatal. She does *not* have a personality disorder, a psychotic disorder, a substance use disorder in the classical sense, a mood disorder, or PTSD. She is a fundamentally healthy person carrying a specific, locatable, severe wound, who has built a coherent and largely pro-social life *around* that wound rather than collapsing into it. The clinical work remaining is the development of a self distinct from the wound — the question of who she would be if her father had never died, never returned, never died again, and never returned again. That question has not yet been answered in canon. The favorable prognosis is that she appears, in recent material, to have begun asking it.

*End psychiatric assessment.*


## Behavioral Atlas

# BEHAVIORAL ATLAS — SUBJECT: CASSANDRA E. LANG

*Compiled from longitudinal observation across the published record (1979–present), with cross-reference to combat footage, domestic surveillance approximations, peer-witness statements (Kaplan, Altman, Barton-K., Kang-N./Iron Lad, Vision-Jonas), and parental record (S. Lang, M. Lang-Paxton).*

The aim of this atlas is the cataloging of the small things: the involuntary motions, the verbal habits, the micro-decisions that occur below the threshold of her own awareness. The Subject is, by temperament, a behavioral processor — she externalizes affect through action rather than rumination — which makes her unusually legible. Almost everything she feels appears, in some form, in her body within seconds. The trained observer can read her in real time.

---

## 1. BASELINE / CALM STATE

**Posture.** At rest, Subject sits forward. Not slumped, not erect — leaning into the room by roughly ten to fifteen degrees from vertical, weight on the balls of the feet even when seated, as if any conversation might at any moment require her to stand and go. This is a forward-orientation that has persisted across every era of the record, including pre-powered childhood appearances. It is not vigilance. It is *availability*.

**Hands.** Almost never still. Default resting positions include: thumb running along the seam of whatever garment is to hand; fingers tracing the edge of a table; one hand cupped around the opposite elbow in a self-soothing half-embrace. When seated for any length of time she will eventually find a small object — a coin, a stylus, a Pym disc, a coffee stirrer — and rotate it through her fingers. Frequency increases with distraction; the rotation is the index of how much of her attention is elsewhere.

**Speech at baseline.** Quick, warm, slightly over-articulated. Subject speaks at approximately 180–200 words per minute in unguarded conversation, faster than the Avengers cohort mean. Verbal tics: liberal use of *"okay, so—"* as a sentence opener, particularly when she is shifting topics; *"right?"* as a tag question seeking peer alignment; a softened *"I mean—"* hedge before a strong opinion. She apologizes pre-emptively for opinions she fully intends to defend — a verbal curtsy before a strike.

**Laugh.** Sharp, surprised-sounding, slightly louder than she expects. Frequently followed by a hand to the mouth — a small abashed gesture inherited from childhood, retained into adulthood. She laughs at her own jokes a half-second after delivering them, which suggests she is hearing them as the room hears them, not pre-composing.

**Eye behavior.** High contact, but in a particular pattern: she looks at faces while listening and breaks contact while speaking, returning to the face only at the key word of a sentence. This is the verbal pattern of someone who learned to read adult moods early — she is checking, while she speaks, that the message is landing.

**Movement habits in domestic space.** Tends to occupy the kitchen rather than the living room when at her father's apartment — proximity to him while he cooks, ambient closeness that does not require sustained dialogue. Tends to occupy thresholds (doorways, the edges of rooms) rather than couches when in unfamiliar homes; sits down only after invitation. At her mother's house she goes immediately to a specific chair and has done so since childhood.

**Sleep.** Light sleeper. Reports (per Kaplan, dorm-shared period) of waking at small noises and remaining awake for upwards of an hour afterward. Sleeps on her side, curled, often with one hand under the pillow — a posture of small, defended self-containment incongruous with her power set. She does not sleep on her back even when injured; the supine position appears to be aversive.

**Eating.** Eats quickly when alone, slowly and conversationally when with others. Has a documented preference for foods her father cooked or ordered during her childhood — specifically grilled cheese, diner-style breakfast, the chain pizza they used to share. Will order these even at restaurants where they are conspicuously inferior options. The food is a route, not a meal.

**Phone behavior.** Checks her phone in a specific tic: thumb-swipe to home, glance, return to pocket, without unlocking. Per peer report this occurs at roughly four-to-seven minute intervals during downtime. The check is for Scott. She does not articulate this; the data is the data.

---

## 2. MILD STRESS / FRICTION

**First indicator: the hands stop.** The fidgeting object is set down. Stillness in the Subject's hands is, counterintuitively, the earliest reliable sign of elevation. She is now attending fully to whatever has snagged her.

**Second indicator: the *"okay, so—"* becomes *"no, okay, but—"*.** She has shifted from receiving the conversation to redirecting it.

**Third indicator: a brief, downward glance.** Specifically to her own wrist or the back of her hand. Surveillance review across multiple incidents shows this glance recurring; functional analysis suggests she is briefly orienting to her own body, possibly an internalized check for size-state. It is the size-changer's version of touching one's pocket for a wallet — *am I still where I think I am.*

**Verbal compensation.** Under mild social friction Subject tends to deploy humor — often self-deprecating, often about her age or her size, occasionally about her death. The death jokes are the most reliable diagnostic for who she trusts: she only makes them with people she has decided are inside the perimeter. Strangers who hear a casual reference to "the last time I died" are being tested. Her read of how they handle it determines admission.

**With peers (Young Avengers cohort):** mild stress produces increased physical contact — a hand on Kaplan's arm, leaning shoulder-to-shoulder with Altman, an unconscious knee-to-knee proximity with whichever Kate-figure is present. She regulates through contact with chosen kin.

**With authority figures:** mild stress produces increased verbal density. She talks more, faster, with more clauses. The talking is a tactic — she is filling space so that the authority cannot deploy a directive into it. The longer the monologue, the more she is rejecting whatever was about to be said to her.

**With her mother:** mild stress produces *quiet*. She shortens. She becomes monosyllabic. The frictional content is almost always Scott-adjacent, and Maggie's accumulated grievance against Scott is something Cassie cannot argue with and cannot endorse. Silence is the only available position.

---

## 3. ACUTE STRESS / IMMINENT THREAT

**Pre-engagement micro-routine.** Approximately two to three seconds before initiating size-change in a combat scenario, Subject performs a consistent sequence: exhale through the nose, drop the shoulders deliberately, plant the dominant foot (right) slightly forward, and flex the fingers of both hands once. This is a self-cued readiness routine, almost certainly developed in adolescence as scaffolding for Pym-particle activation and now autonomic. It survives across costume eras (Stature, Stinger, Giant-Girl). The flex is the most reliable tell — even out of costume, in a tense civilian situation, the fingers will do it.

**Size-state choice under threat.** Subject's default escalation is *upward*. Given a free choice in a hostile environment she will go large before she goes small, even when smallness would be tactically superior. The bias is so consistent it qualifies as a signature. Going small is a deliberate, second-thought option for her, typically chosen only when she has been explicitly tasked or when a teammate is operating large and she is taking the complementary role. The unconscious preference for largeness is the foundational psychological pattern made operational: she does not want to be hard to see. She wants to be *unavoidable*.

**Voice under threat.** Drops in pitch by a perceptible interval. The rapid, warm baseline gives way to a flatter, slower delivery — fewer hedges, no apologies, declarative sentences. The *"I mean—"* disappears entirely. This is the closest the Subject ever comes to sounding like her father in his operational mode.

**Eye behavior under threat.** Reverses. She now holds contact while speaking (issuing direction or threat) and breaks it while listening (assessing the room). The reversal is so consistent it can be used as a check on whether the Subject is genuinely in combat-mode or performing.

**Cardiovascular note.** Sustained large-form combat places the Subject under significant cardiac load (see Medical). Observable correlates: a slight pallor that develops after roughly ninety seconds at high-end size; a tendency to drop back to baseline scale briefly between engagements, ostensibly tactical but with a recovery function; a habit of pressing the heel of the hand briefly against the sternum during these resets. The sternum-press is the most clinically significant tell in her repertoire. She does it without apparent awareness. It indicates she is monitoring her own heart and would deny doing so if asked.

**Tactical decision habits under acute threat:**
- Will move toward the largest concentration of enemies rather than the most dangerous single enemy. Crowd control is her comfort zone.
- Will reliably interpose herself between a threat and a smaller/younger teammate. The protective bias is absolute.
- Will hesitate, by a measurable fraction of a second, before striking an opponent who has visibly disengaged or surrendered. This hesitation has been exploited at least twice in the published record.
- Will not pursue a fleeing opponent if a teammate is wounded. Triage instinct beats pursuit instinct.

---

## 4. INTIMACY / TRUST BEHAVIORS

**The admission ladder.** Subject inducts new people into trust through a recognizable sequence: (1) shared humor at her own expense; (2) a small, testable confidence ("don't tell my dad I—"); (3) a death joke; (4) eventually, an unguarded reference to Scott in the present tense. Reaching stage four indicates she has structurally incorporated the person into her chosen-family map. The induction can take weeks or, with the right cue, twenty minutes.

**Physical intimacy with peers.** High-contact, non-romantic, default-platonic. She leans, drapes, perches. With Kaplan she has a documented habit of resting her chin on his shoulder when standing behind him; with Altman she sits cross-legged with one knee pressing against his. The contact is regulatory, not sexual; she touches the people she has decided are safe in order to verify they are still there.

**Romantic intimacy.** Tends to choose partners whose ontological status is unstable — the synthetic, the time-displaced, the returned-from-loss. With these partners she displays a specific behavioral set: increased verbal earnestness (the hedges drop), heightened attention to the partner's physical continuity (touching the chest, the wrist, the face — verifying the body is present), and a tendency to ask questions about memory and identity that would seem invasive in a more conventional pairing but are, for her, the central diagnostic of the relationship. *Are you still you. Are you still here.* The questions are about herself as much as about him.

**Conflict with intimates.** Subject does not stonewall and does not explode. Her conflict mode is *prolonged earnest discussion*, sometimes lasting hours, in which she will return repeatedly to the same point with slightly different framings until the other person engages with it. She does not raise her voice. She does raise her stakes — escalating from "I'm upset" to "this is important to me" to, in the rare extreme case, "this is the kind of thing I don't recover from." The escalation is honest, not manipulative; she reports her internal state in real time.

**Apology behavior.** Apologizes verbosely and concretely. Will name the specific harm, the specific reason she caused it, and the specific change she will make. Has a tendency to apologize for things that are not her fault, particularly in domestic contexts with her mother. Distinguishing the structural over-apology from a meaningful apology requires attending to the second clause: meaningful apologies include the *reason*; reflexive ones do not.

**With her father, specifically.** Two coexisting registers. *Performative*: light teasing, eye-roll, ironic "Dad" with three syllables — the rehearsed daughter role. *Beneath that*: any time Scott exits a room, she watches him go all the way out. Any time he returns, she clocks his entry within two seconds even if she is mid-conversation facing the other way. Any time he is on a comm channel she will physically still herself to listen. The performative layer is for the room; the watching is for her.

---

## 5. GRIEF STATES

**Acute grief (fresh loss).** Subject becomes physically smaller — not via Pym-modulation, but via posture. Shoulders fold, head drops, the forward lean becomes a curl. The forward-availability of her baseline collapses inward. Hands return to fidgeting at an elevated rate; specifically, she will hold an object in both hands and not put it down for extended periods.

**Voice in acute grief.** Quiet, even-toned, with a noticeable tendency toward present-tense and second-person references to the lost. *"He always says—"* not *"he always said."* The slippage is involuntary and persistent. When corrected by another speaker she does not adjust; she simply stops talking.

**Behavioral compensation.** She works. Specifically, she performs caretaking labor for the people around her — cooking, cleaning, organizing the team space, taking over communications. Grief converts directly into logistics. This is observable and has been so since adolescence; the Children's Crusade record shows her organizing supply runs and patrols in the immediate aftermath of multiple casualties.

**The reunion vulnerability.** Subject's largest operational liability is her response to offers of reunion with the dead. When such an offer is made — by an apparent resurrection, a time-displacement, a magical bargain, an obvious trap — she will accept it. Not impulsively: she will frequently articulate the suspicion that it is a trap, then accept anyway. The decision is conscious. She has stated, in substance and on multiple occasions, that she would rather be hurt by a false reunion than fail a true one. This is the single most exploitable trait in her behavioral profile and any intelligence-grade actor with knowledge of her history can predict her response to such a stimulus with high confidence.

**Chronic / background grief.** The default grief state — which is approximately always present at low amplitude — manifests as: the phone-check tic; the diner food choices; sleeping curled; talking to her father, in conversation with others, with a frequency disproportionate to topical relevance. She brings him up roughly every twelve to fifteen minutes in casual conversation, per peer report. She does not notice this. When it is pointed out gently she will laugh — the small abashed laugh — and try to track it for a few minutes, fail, and let it go.

**Anniversary behaviors.** Subject becomes withdrawn and slightly irritable in the days surrounding the anniversary of the original Avengers Mansion incident. She does not always know why; her mother has, on occasion, had to remind her of the date. The body remembers what the calendar does not. She tends to seek out children's media (specific cartoons from her own childhood) during these windows. Regression as self-soothing.

---

## 6. VICTORY STATES

**Immediate post-combat.** Subject does not celebrate at the site. She performs accounting: counts teammates, verifies civilians, confirms the threat is down. Only after the count is complete does she allow the affect to register. The accounting is non-negotiable and has cost her, in at least one engagement, a moment of vulnerability when she stopped to check on a teammate before the threat was truly neutralized.

**Celebratory mode.** When the team wins cleanly, Subject becomes notably louder, more physical, more tactile. She will hug, she will high-five, she will make declarative jokes with full volume. The post-victory hour is the one window in which her speech rate exceeds baseline and her hedges fully disappear. This is the closest she gets to unguarded happiness, and it is reliably brief — within an hour or two she will have re-collapsed to baseline as the awareness reasserts itself that someone, somewhere, is still in danger or grieving or dead.

**With her father, post-victory.** A specific behavior: she will, at some point, find Scott and stand beside him without speaking, often shoulder-to-shoulder, both of them facing the same direction. The non-conversational adjacency is the celebration. She does not require him to say anything. She requires him to be there to stand next to.

**Trophies, mementos.** Keeps few. The exception: small physical objects associated with her father's history — an old Ant-Man helmet component, a Pym disc from a particular mission, a coin from a place they visited. These are stored in a single container she carries through moves. They are not displayed; they are *kept*.

---

## 7. DEFEAT STATES

**Acute tactical defeat.** Subject's first behavior after a loss is to ask whether anyone is hurt. The second is to ask what she could have done differently. The third — and this is consistent across runs — is to volunteer for the next, more dangerous, follow-up engagement. Defeat increases her appetite for risk in the short term. The pattern is counterphobic and has been remarked upon by teammates with concern.

**Verbal markers.** In defeat she speaks in the first person plural ("we should have," "we missed") even when the failure was demonstrably individual. She refuses to allocate blame to teammates and will absorb it onto herself with a speed that has occasionally prevented genuine after-action analysis. Leaders attempting to do real tactical review have to specifically block her from over-claiming responsibility.

**Withdrawal pattern.** When a defeat is large enough to penetrate the busy-work compensation — a death, a strategic catastrophe — Subject will physically leave the team space for periods of hours to days. Destinations are predictable: her father's apartment, her childhood home, or a small set of low-traffic outdoor locations (rooftops, a specific waterfront, a particular cemetery). She does not hide; she is reachable; she simply requires solitude in a place that contains a memory.

**Rumination, when it occurs.** Subject is not a natural ruminator, but defeat involving the death of a peer will produce a recognizable cycle: she will replay the engagement at increasing levels of detail, articulating each decision point aloud (to herself or a trusted listener), looking for the place she could have intervened differently. The cycle terminates not in resolution but in exhaustion. She will then sleep heavily, often for ten-plus hours, and wake to a noticeably lifted baseline. The cycle has therapeutic function for her and should not be interrupted by well-meaning intervention.

**The catastrophic defeat — her own death.** The record contains one. Her behavior in the seconds before — choosing to engage Doom rather than withdraw, with her resurrected father present and the team in disarray — is the diagnostic case of her behavior under existential pressure. Given a choice between self-preservation and the reunion project, she selected the reunion project. The decision was made in under a second and was, by every available indicator, unhesitating. Any future operational profile must assume that under sufficiently freighted circumstances she will repeat the choice.

---

## 8. ROUTES, RITUALS, AND HABITS

**Movement habits in the city.** Walks rather than takes transit when distance permits. Prefers surface streets to rooftops in daily movement (rooftop transit is reserved for operational contexts). Has approximately three preferred coffee establishments, rotated by neighborhood; orders the same drink at each (medium drip, room for cream). Tips above the tax line, in cash, reliably.

**Phone and digital habits.** Texts in complete sentences with capitalization and terminal punctuation — a generationally atypical pattern that suggests early adoption of texting under parental supervision (a mother-enforced norm, per inference). Uses voice notes with her father and only her father. Photographs food and small everyday objects more than people. Maintains a thin social media presence with long silent periods.

**Wardrobe.** Defaults to layers — a base tee, an overshirt or hoodie, accessible boots. The layered preference is consistent with someone who has had her clothes destroyed by size-change enough times to value modular, replaceable garments. Color palette skews warm and earth-toned. Has a small number of items she rotates heavily rather than a large wardrobe.

**Pre-mission ritual.** Roughly fifteen minutes before deploying she will go quiet, separate from the group, and perform a personal check that includes: verifying her suit's seals (touch each closure in sequence), confirming her comm, and, last, sending Scott a short message. The message is sometimes substantive, frequently banal; its function is the sending, not the content. If Scott is on the mission with her she will instead make eye contact with him, briefly, and nod. The ritual has not been observed to be skippable; on occasions when external pressure has prevented it she has been measurably less composed in the opening minutes of engagement.

**Post-mission ritual.** Showers immediately upon returning to base. Eats — often the diner-style comfort food noted above. Calls or visits her father within twenty-four hours regardless of whether he was on the mission. Sleeps. The sequence is rigid.

**Annual / seasonal patterns.** Birthday is observed quietly and with mild ambivalence (resurrection-related age confusion produces an unsettled relationship to her own chronology). Father's Day is observed with disproportionate intensity — gift-giving, scheduled presence, multi-day visits. Holidays are split between Maggie's household (obligation, executed warmly but with the shortened verbal register noted above) and Scott's (preferred, looser, longer).

---

## 9. TELLS UNDER DECEPTION

**When she is lying:** Subject is a poor liar in low-stakes social contexts and a competent one in high-stakes operational contexts — a useful inversion. In low-stakes lies she over-elaborates, providing detail that was not requested, and her speech rate climbs above baseline. The hedges return in dense clusters. In high-stakes operational deception she flattens — her voice drops to the threat-state register noted above, her hands still — and she becomes briefly, uncharacteristically, hard to read. The contrast itself is a tell: a Cassie Lang who is unreadable is a Cassie Lang who is operating under stakes she considers serious.

**When she is being lied to:** She typically knows. She does not always say so. Her response to detected deception is, in order: a brief eye-narrow, a single small head-tilt, and then a decision — either to challenge directly (if the deceiver is inside her trust perimeter and she believes the confrontation can be productive) or to file the data and proceed (if the deceiver is outside it). The filing decision is the more common one. She remembers the lie. She does not forget that she was lied to. The grace period extended to a detected liar is generous in the moment and ungenerous over time.

---

## 10. WHAT THE ATLAS DOES NOT CONTAIN

A complete behavioral atlas would presume a complete subject. The Subject is, by her own implicit assessment, unfinished — the third Cassie, the one underneath both the daughter and the hero, has not yet been fully observed by herself or by anyone close to her. The behaviors catalogued here are the behaviors of a person mid-construction, whose habits were formed in service of two organizing projects (be near the father, become large enough to matter) that may, in the long arc, give way to something else. The atlas is therefore a snapshot of a moving figure. The patterns are stable; the person inside them is, on the available evidence, still becoming.

*— end Behavioral Atlas. Cross-reference: Medical (cardiac load), Psychological (counterphobic organization), Pressure Points (reunion v


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# DOSSIER SECTION — DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS

*Subject: Cassandra E. Lang. Compiled from longitudinal observation across published canon (1979–present), reconciled across writers and timelines. What follows is the granular texture of a life — the temporal architecture inside which the psychology runs.*

---

## 1. THE SHAPE OF A WEEK

A week of surveillance on Subject would, at first pass, look unremarkable for a young woman in her late teens/early twenties living in a mid-density American city (typical operational base: Coral Gables / greater Miami during Scott's Astonishing tenure; New York during Young Avengers rotations; intermittent Los Angeles, Cresskill NJ, and the Lang/Paxton family home depending on era). She moves between three habitats — *father's residence, mother's residence, and team space* — and the relative weighting of those three is the single most diagnostic indicator of her current emotional state. When she is well, the three are balanced. When she is grieving, hurt, or destabilized, she gravitates toward Scott's place and toward the team. When she is angry at Scott specifically (rare but documented — Astonishing Ant-Man, post-incarceration), she retreats to Maggie's.

Mornings are slow and protracted; she is not a dawn riser unless a mission requires it. Mid-mornings are her productive cognitive window. Afternoons are physical — training, patrol, errands. Evenings are social — team contact, calls, screens. Late nights are her private hours, the time she protects most jealously, and the time during which the surveillance-relevant behaviors emerge.

---

## 2. MORNINGS

**Wake.** Subject does not use a single alarm; she uses a stacked set (typically three, at five- to seven-minute intervals), and snoozes the first two on principle. This is a small but consistent tell — she resists the imperative to *get up at the named time*; she prefers to negotiate the morning into existence on her own terms. When she finally rises it is decisively, not gradually; once vertical she does not return to bed.

**First check.** Phone, in this order, observed consistently: (1) messages from Scott, (2) the team group thread, (3) news headlines scanned for Avengers-adjacent incidents and for any reportage involving her father's name or known associates, (4) social. The Scott-check is automatic and pre-cognitive; she does it before her eyes are fully open. On any morning where Scott has *not* messaged in 18+ hours, her affect across the rest of the day measurably contracts — quieter, more vigilant, more prone to initiate the contact herself by midmorning.

**Body check.** Subject runs a brief, near-unconscious somatic inventory on rising: a hand to the sternum (legacy of the cardiac history — she palpates without naming what she is doing), a stretch, a shallow size-test.
