The size-test is the most distinctive ritual on the file. She will, in private, microscale — a few inches up, a few inches down, and back to baseline — to confirm the Pym integration is responsive. It takes under two seconds and she does it without looking. It is the same gesture another person might use to crack their knuckles or roll their neck. She has done it daily, by inference, since approximately age fourteen.

**Hygiene and dress.** Quick. Subject does not linger at mirrors. Hair is functional — pulled back when in field gear, loose otherwise; she will cut it herself when impatient with appointments. Wardrobe runs to soft athletic layers, jeans, hoodies in dark or jewel tones, and a small rotation of items belonging to or associated with Scott (a worn flannel, an oversized t-shirt she has had since middle school by the resurrection-reset chronology — though the math on its actual age is incoherent and she does not pursue it). She owns very few "nice" clothes and treats events requiring them as foreign assignments. Footwear: predominantly low-profile sneakers in good condition, replaced before they are visibly worn — a quiet operational discipline; she does not run patrols in compromised shoes.

**Breakfast.** Inconsistent. When at Scott's: whatever Scott is making, often pancakes or eggs, eaten while talking. When at Maggie's: cereal or toast, eaten while reading. When alone or at team housing: coffee and something portable. Subject has a documented preference for sweet over savory in the morning and a near-clinical relationship with coffee — two cups, always, the first within twenty minutes of waking, the second mid-morning. She drinks it with milk and too much sugar, a habit her stepfather once tried to correct and which she has therefore kept on principle.

---

## 3. THE DAYTIME RHYTHM

Subject's daytime varies sharply by era — student, intern, hero-on-payroll, full-time operator — but the underlying *rhythm* is preserved.

**Civilian work / study block (mid-morning to early afternoon).** During school-attending periods she is a competent if uneven student: strong in humanities and social sciences, capable in math, indifferent to chemistry, deeply interested in physics (specifically anything touching size, scale, or particle behavior — a self-directed obsession that predates her formal education in it). She takes notes by hand, in pen, in a stack of cheap composition notebooks she does not throw away. She has been documented re-reading her own old notebooks late at night, especially ones from the year of Scott's first death; this is a private grief ritual she does not name.

During Scott's security-company era she works alongside him in a junior capacity — answering phones, running errands, sitting in on operations she is technically too young for and which Scott pretends not to notice her attending. She is good at the work and her father trusts her with cash, keys, and codes, which she registers and reciprocates by being scrupulously trustworthy in those specific domains. (She will lie to him about her own safety. She will not lie to him about the books.)

**Movement.** Subject walks more than her peers. She prefers to walk routes she could otherwise drive or transit; she calls it *thinking time*. Surveillance would record consistent pedestrian loops — to a coffee shop, to a bookstore or library, through a park, back. She varies the route only modestly; she likes the familiar. She is not formally counter-surveillance conscious in civilian movement, which is itself useful intelligence — she does not believe she is being watched, which means she has not been seriously targeted, which means her threat model is built around enemies of *Scott*, not enemies of *Cassie*. This is a vulnerability she has not yet updated.

**Phone discipline.** She texts more than she calls. She calls Scott. She texts everyone else. She uses voice notes with her closest peers (the Young Avengers cohort) — long, rambling, frequently funny voice notes that she sends and forgets, and which constitute her most natural mode of self-disclosure. A surveillance package recovering her voice-note history would find more of her interior life there than in any journal.

**Lunch.** Frequently skipped or grazed. Subject eats irregularly during the day and compensates at dinner. Her appetite is robust when she is happy and contracts visibly under stress — particularly Scott-themed stress. Documented preferences: pizza (slice, not whole; she likes a single slice eaten standing up), tacos, ramen, anything involving a sauce she can dip in. Documented avoidances: shellfish (mild distrust, not allergy), most fish, beets. She drinks water inconsistently — a household joke between her and Scott, who nags her about it; this is one of the few domains in which she lets him father her without resistance.

---

## 4. THE TRAINING & PATROL BLOCK

**Physical training.** Subject trains four to six days a week, with intensity scaling by current operational tempo. Components in observed rotation:

- *Cardio* (running, primarily; she is a long-loose-stride runner, not a sprinter). She runs with music — high-energy, era-appropriate, a playlist she has had since her early teens with additions but no deletions, which is itself diagnostic of her relationship to her own past.
- *Strength* (functional, not aesthetic; she trains to be useful at multiple scales, which means her programming includes both unloaded plyometric work — useful when small — and heavy compound lifts — useful when large). She is stronger than she looks at baseline size and weaker than she should be at giant size relative to mass, a discrepancy she works to close.
- *Size drills* in private space. This is the ritual no one else does. She practices transitions — small to baseline, baseline to large, large to small to large — for tempo, control, and proprioceptive integration. She trains the *timing* of size change as a martial art unto itself. Observation: she practices micro-transitions (inches) far more than dramatic ones. The dramatic transitions are easy. The control is hard.
- *Sparring* with team members, with Scott when available, occasionally with Hank Pym in eras where that relationship is operative. She is a willing learner and a poor loser; she does not sulk, but she remembers, and she will revisit a technique that beat her until she can defend it.

**Cardiac discipline.** Subject monitors her own heart rate during exertion in a way she does not advertise. She wears a fitness band she pretends is for steps. She checks her pulse in elevators, in waiting rooms, at the end of long size-change sequences. She has never, on record, asked a cardiologist about the cumulative effect of Pym dosing on her repaired heart. The avoidance is the data point — she does not want to know.

**Patrol / response.** When operationally active she runs a patrol pattern that prioritizes responsiveness over presence: she does not "walk a beat" so much as stage herself in a location with sightlines and comms and wait. She prefers high vantage to street level, which is partly tactical (she scales up easily; she scales up *down* less easily) and partly temperamental — she likes to see the whole shape of a thing before entering it. She has been documented sitting for forty minutes on a rooftop doing essentially nothing, eating something out of a wrapper, watching the city. These quiet stretches are when she does her actual processing.

---

## 5. EVENINGS

**Dinner.** The most consistent meal of her day, and the one most likely to be social. With Scott: cooked at home or takeout, eaten at a table, conversation expected. With the team: communal, chaotic, frequently a shared pizza order or a group cooking project that goes sideways. Alone: the largest meal of her day, often eaten in front of a screen. She eats relatively quickly and is not precious about food; she will, however, eat the components of a meal in a particular order (savories first, anything sweet or sauced last) — a mild structural preference she has had since childhood.

**Social contact.** Evenings are when she answers the day's accumulated messages, returns voice notes, and participates in the running group threads. She is a connector: she will introduce people in her network to each other, remember birthdays, remember anniversaries of losses, and send the message no one else thought to send. This is not performance; it is the *holding* function her psychology has assigned her. Her thumb-typing is fast and her punctuation is loose; she uses ellipses heavily.

**Screen habits.** Subject watches television in a peculiar way: she will re-watch known-comforting content rather than seek new content, especially when stressed. A surveillance file would record long stretches of the same sitcoms, the same animated series she watched as a child, the same handful of films. She is a re-reader as well — she has a small shelf of books she returns to. New media she consumes socially, with the team, where the conversation around it is as important as the thing itself. She plays video games competently and intermittently; she is better at cooperative than competitive formats, which tracks.

**The Scott call.** Almost every evening she does not see him in person, she calls Scott. The call is brief (typically 6–14 minutes), routinized in content (his day, her day, a joke, a verification that he is alive), and the most ritualized event in her week. She places it; he answers. If he does not answer within the hour she will call again. If he does not answer the second time her affect destabilizes measurably and she will begin to ping his known associates within ninety minutes. This is the single most reliable behavioral predictor in the file: *the duration between an unanswered call to Scott and the initiation of a welfare check is under two hours.*

---

## 6. NIGHTS — THE PRIVATE HOURS

This is the period of greatest diagnostic value and the period least visible to the people in her life.

**The wind-down is long.** Subject does not sleep on a schedule. She will be in bed by midnight on training nights, awake until two or three on others. She reads on her phone in the dark, scrolls, sometimes writes — she keeps a sporadic journal, never daily, that surfaces at irregular intervals and is concentrated around anniversaries (Scott's first death, her own death-and-return, the Children's Crusade dates). The journal is not introspective in the therapeutic sense; it is more often *letters to Scott* written in a register that assumes he might read them, even when she knows he will not.

**The grief windows.** On documented anniversaries — and on dates the surveillance file would learn to flag by repetition — her late-night behavior shifts. She will re-read old text threads. She will look at photographs. She has been observed, during these windows, performing the size-test more often than at other times, almost as fidget — confirming the *thing the loss made her into* is still there and still works. She does not cry easily, but she cries in these windows, alone, briefly, and then sleeps.

**Sleep.** Subject sleeps hard once she is under, and dreams vividly. She is a side-sleeper, fully under a heavy blanket regardless of climate; she runs cold at baseline size and has commented on it. She has had recurring sleep disturbance documented in canon around traumatic anniversaries — not nightmare disorder, but parasomnic episodes consistent with grief-state physiology. She does not use sleep aids.

**Nocturnal calls.** A subset of evenings she does not call Scott — she calls one of two or three peers in the Young Avengers cohort, late, and talks for an hour or more about nothing in particular. These calls are her primary mode of emotional regulation outside the father-axis. The recipients have learned to answer.

---

## 7. HABITS OF PLACE

**Preferred environments.** Subject is drawn to places with multiple sightlines, soft light, and the option to leave quickly. She likes corner booths, window seats, rooftops, parks with multiple exits. She does not like windowless rooms and dislikes basements categorically — a preference she does not connect to anything specific but which is consistent with a person who once died underground in a citadel.

**Coffee shops.** She has a *one* coffee shop in any city she occupies for more than a week. She develops it within days. She tips well, learns the names of the staff, and orders consistently. This is a stabilizing ritual; she imports it wherever she goes.

**The bookstore / library habit.** Subject reads physical books in addition to her phone. She browses bookstores as an activity in itself, often without buying anything; she handles books, reads first pages, puts them back. Libraries she uses for quiet — she will sit and not-read in a library for an hour. The pattern is consistent across cities and eras.

**Home space.** Her personal spaces are *layered*, not curated: posters and prints from across her life still on the walls (she does not redecorate; she accretes), a desk with stacks rather than files, a bed made loosely, photographs taped rather than framed, an asymmetric distribution of belongings that suggests she has never fully unpacked from any move. Her belongings include disproportionately many items associated with her father — concert tickets they attended together, a coin from a job he pulled before she was born and gave her as a half-joke half-confession, the flannel previously noted. She would resist describing these as a shrine. They function as one.

**The size of the rooms she chooses.** A small, repeated, possibly unconscious preference: Subject gravitates to *larger* rooms when given the choice — apartments with high ceilings, gyms with open floors, restaurants with airy interiors. She is uncomfortable in low-ceilinged spaces in a way she has not articulated. For a person whose body can occupy any volume, the preference is overdetermined; she wants the room to accommodate her largest self even when she has no intention of getting large.

---

## 8. RITUALS — NAMED AND UNNAMED

A non-exhaustive catalog of the recurring small ceremonies that structure her interior life:

- **The morning size-test.** Daily, two seconds, never skipped.
- **The sternum touch.** Multiple times a day, unconscious, always under stress.
- **The evening Scott call.** Near-daily, near-sacred.
- **The route walk.** Whenever she needs to think, she walks the same loop in the current city.
- **The notebook re-read.** Sporadic, late-night, grief-windowed.
- **The anniversary journaling.** Annual, brief, letter-form.
- **The team thread check.** Every morning, every evening, multiple times daily under stress.
- **The pre-mission ritual.** A specific sequence before any operation: a text to Scott (always the same general content — some version of *love you, see you later*, never *be careful*, which she superstitiously avoids because it sounds like a goodbye), a check of equipment, a single full size cycle (small to large to baseline) as a system test, and a slow exhale she does not know she is doing.
- **The post-mission ritual.** A long shower, sweet food, and screen time with comfort content. She does not debrief immediately; she processes after sleep.
- **The Wanda question.** Periodically — perhaps twice a year, unprompted — she will mention Wanda Maximoff in conversation with someone who knows the history, in a flat tone, asking how she is. She has forgiven; she has not forgotten; she is monitoring the forgiveness.

---

## 9. WHAT A WEEK OF SURVEILLANCE WOULD ACTUALLY RECORD

Compressed: a young woman who wakes slowly, drinks too much coffee, walks more than necessary, trains hard in private and harder in company, eats irregularly and socially, talks to her father every day, holds her chosen family together by text and voice, watches the same shows she watched as a kid, reads in libraries she does not need to be in, runs the same loops in any city, sleeps deep and dreams loudly, writes letters to a dead man she has buried twice and welcomed back once, palpates her own chest without knowing she is doing it, tests the size of her body every morning to confirm it still belongs to her, and protects with significant operational rigor the few hours each day in which she is not performing daughter, hero, teammate, or friend — the hours in which she is attempting, slowly and without instruction, to find out who she is when none of those people are looking.

That last project — visible only in the negative space of the schedule, in what she does not do and where she goes when she has nowhere she has to be — is the most important pattern in the file.

*End section. Continue to next.*


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*Subject: Cassandra Eleanor "Cassie" Lang. Section scope: blood family, step-family, the unborn-but-formative, and the dead who retain operational presence in her interior life. Each profile traces the bond from earliest panel to current canonical state, with attention to micro-behavioral tells (how she greets them, where she stands in a room with them, what subjects she will and will not raise), trust topology, predictable failure modes, and the leverage any hostile actor would gain through them.*

---

## 1. SCOTT EDWARD HARRIS LANG — FATHER (Ant-Man II)

The central relationship of the subject's entire life. Every other bond in this dossier is, to some degree, organized in relation to this one.

### History

The relationship begins in extremis. Cassie is a sick child — congenital cardiac defect, prognosis poor without specialist intervention — when Scott, recently paroled from San Quentin and employed by Stark International as an electronics technician, makes the decision to steal the Pym Particles and the Ant-Man suit to extract Dr. Erica Sondheim from Darren Cross's custody. The originating fact of Cassie's conscious existence: *Daddy went to prison once, and Daddy will go again, because of me.* She is told a sanitized version as a small child; she learns the full version in stages through adolescence; by Young Avengers era she has fully integrated it. Her response is not guilt — it is *valorization*. Scott's recidivism becomes, in her internal mythology, the originating heroic act.

Through the 1980s and early 1990s she is the domestic anchor of Scott's increasingly transient life — his FF tenure, his Heroes for Hire stint, his Avengers period. Custody is split with her mother Maggie; visitation is irregular because of Scott's professional volatility. Cassie does not experience this as neglect. She experiences it as *waiting*, and she becomes very good at waiting — a behavioral pattern that recurs across decades. (Surveillance note: when expecting Scott, the subject historically positions herself near a window or doorway approximately 15-20 minutes before announced arrival; she does not relax her posture until visual confirmation. This pre-arrival vigil persists into adulthood and is visible even when she is ostensibly engaged in other tasks.)

The Avengers Disassembled event (2004) ruptures the relationship for the first time and structures it forever after. Scott is killed in the Kree warship strike at Avengers Mansion. Cassie watches on television — the specific somatic memory she returns to in later monologues is *the rerun footage*, the loop, watching it twice before the announcer confirmed the names of the dead. She was approximately 10-12. There was no body recovered. There was no funeral that contained him; there was a memorial.

The Young Avengers era is, in unambiguous psychological terms, the construction of a relationship with a dead man. Cassie's Pym dosing (covert, escalating, beginning months before his death and intensifying after) is not preparation for heroism in the abstract — it is preparation for *being him*. She moves through the world after 2004 as a delegate, a continuation, a kept promise.

Children's Crusade returns him to her briefly. The reunion is brief, intense, and ends with his second death at Doom's hands and her own death seconds later. She dies *beside her father*. There is no other character in Marvel canon for whom death-and-reunion functions this exact way.

Post-resurrection, when Cassie re-enters Scott's life during his Astonishing Ant-Man / security-firm period, the relationship requires reconstruction from both directions. He has grieved her, twice. She has died and returned chronologically younger. They are both, in functional terms, ghosts to each other for the first weeks. The current bond — by the most recent runs — has matured: Cassie loves Scott as a flawed, recovering, frequently embarrassing man rather than as a martyred ideal. This maturation is the single healthiest development in her psychological record.

### Dynamics — micro-pattern

- **Greeting ritual:** Physical contact immediately and without negotiation — hug initiated by her, sustained 1-2 seconds longer than Scott reciprocates. She has done this every time since resurrection; she did not do this consistently before Disassembled. The hug is the live confirmation that he is corporeal.
- **Speech register:** With Scott alone, she drops to a slightly younger vocal cadence than her chronological age — pet names ("Dad" lengthened to "Daaaad" when teasing), the use of inside-joke references that predate his first death. With Scott in front of peers, she shifts to a performative exasperation register that allows her to be visibly affectionate while appearing age-appropriately embarrassed.
- **Topic avoidance:** She does not initiate discussion of Disassembled, of Doom, of the period she was dead. She will answer if he asks, but flatly and without elaboration. She does not ask him about the years he grieved her. There is a mutually maintained zone of silence around the worst material.
- **Tactical synchrony:** In combat with Scott, she defers to his calls in the first 30 seconds of any engagement and then operates independently. This is observable across multiple field engagements and is the only domain in which she reflexively accepts his authority.
- **Eating behavior:** When sharing a meal with Scott she eats more, faster, and with less self-consciousness than in any other documented context. He cooks badly; she eats it anyway and praises it. This is *symbolic feeding*, the daughter consuming what the father provides as a continuous reaffirmation that he is here to provide.

### Evolution

Three phases:

**Phase I — Unambiguous Idealization (childhood through Disassembled):** Scott is hero, victim of circumstance, the parent who is fun. Maggie does the work; Scott does the magic. Cassie does not, at this stage, perceive Scott's flaws as flaws.

**Phase II — Posthumous Fusion (Disassembled through Children's Crusade death):** Scott exists as internal object, mission, vocation. Cassie's selfhood is operationally indistinguishable from her commitment to *being him* and *getting him back*. This is the most psychologically dangerous phase.

**Phase III — Reconstructed, Particularized Love (post-resurrection through current):** Scott is a man. He steals. He lies sometimes. He is a mediocre boyfriend to women he genuinely loves. He is broke. He is also funny, kind, brave, and present. Cassie loves him in this granular, disillusioned mode, and the love is stronger for being earned rather than projected.

### Trust / Safety

Trust is total in the affective register and partial in the practical register. She trusts Scott to love her absolutely; she does not trust him to be on time, to manage money, or to tell her the full truth about a job. This is correctly calibrated. She would trust him with her life and would not trust him with her rent.

Safety with Scott is paradoxical — she is emotionally safest in his presence and physically most at risk, because proximity to Scott means proximity to Scott's enemies (Cross, Crossfire, Power Broker, the entire rogues' gallery that views Cassie as the optimal pressure point on Ant-Man). She has internalized this and does not resent it. The risk is the price of the bond.

### Failure Mode

The failure mode is *self-sacrifice for father-reunion*. Demonstrated, lethal, repeated. Given a credible offer of recovering Scott from death, separation, or capture, the subject will spend any resource including her own life. She has done so once already.

Secondary failure mode: when Scott is captured, missing, or under threat, the subject's tactical judgment degrades sharply — she becomes large, loud, declarative, and locatable in ways that compromise stealth-dependent operations.

### Leverage

Maximal. Scott Lang is the single highest-value lever against this subject. Threat to Scott, news of Scott's death (real or fabricated), or apparent opportunity to save Scott will produce predictable, large-scale, often imprudent action. A sophisticated adversary who understands the resurrection pattern of her biography could construct a reunion-trap to which she would walk in voluntarily. *This vector is known; she knows it is known; she will still walk in.*

---

## 2. MARGARET "MAGGIE" LANG (later Paxton) — MOTHER

The most underestimated relationship in the dossier — by Cassie herself, by Scott, by most writers who handle the character, and (if we are honest) by the subject's own psychological economy.

### History

Maggie is Scott's ex-wife, the woman who stayed during his first incarceration and divorced him after his second criminal episode (the Ant-Man theft itself, which she only partially understood at the time and never fully forgave). She is a working-class woman of considerable practical competence — managed Cassie's medical care during the cardiac crisis, navigated insurance and surgical logistics, held a household together on one income through Scott's incarceration. She remarries Jim Paxton, a police officer, and the household becomes structurally hostile to Scott's renewed criminal-adjacent vocation.

Cassie's relationship with Maggie is the *foundational* relationship in every metric except emotional weight. Maggie fed her, bathed her, took her to cardiology appointments, sat with her in pre-op, drove her to school for a decade, and held her on the night Scott died. Cassie knows this intellectually and undervalues it affectively. This is the single most psychologically telling fact about the subject.

### Dynamics — micro-pattern

- **Greeting ritual:** Brief embrace, often initiated by Maggie rather than Cassie. Cassie offers cheek; Maggie kisses cheek. The contact is shorter and more formal than the Scott hug.
- **Speech register:** With Maggie, the subject's voice is *older* than her chronological age — adult-to-adult, sometimes impatient, sometimes tender, rarely playful. She does not use childhood pet names with Maggie even though Maggie used them with her.
- **Conflict pattern:** Disagreement with Maggie produces door-closing, room-leaving, and (during adolescence) physical departure from the house. Cassie does not yell at Maggie; she absents herself. Maggie does not pursue. The conflict resolution mode is gradual reapproach by Cassie 24-72 hours later with a flattened affect that approximates apology without using the word.
- **Topic avoidance:** Cassie will not discuss the Pym dosing with Maggie. Will not discuss combat injuries. Will not discuss Scott in heroic register (only domestic register, neutral tone). The protected zone is enormous.
- **Body language:** Cassie occupies less physical space around Maggie than around any other person in her life. She sits with knees together, hands folded, voice slightly lower in volume. This is the only consistent context in which she actively minimizes her size. The somatic message is *I am being good, I am being small, I am not the daughter who is making this hard.*

### Evolution

Childhood: warm, complete dependency. Maggie is the world.

Adolescence (post-Scott's death): sharp rupture. Cassie experiences Maggie's protective restrictions ("you cannot run off to play hero, you have a heart condition, you have already lost a father") as a betrayal of Scott's memory. She lies, steals Pym particles, sneaks out, and ultimately leaves to join the Young Avengers in direct defiance. Maggie reports her missing; Paxton attempts retrieval; Cassie refuses.

Young adulthood: cool reconciliation. Cassie returns home periodically, observes the rituals, accepts meals, and treats Maggie as a beloved but limited figure — someone who *cannot understand* the life Cassie has chosen and is therefore exempted from real intimacy. This is unfair and Cassie partially knows it.

Post-resurrection: a complicated re-encounter. Maggie has grieved Cassie. Maggie has rebuilt around the loss. Cassie's return does not restore the old relationship — it institutes a third one, more careful, more tender, more aware on both sides of how thin the thread is.

### Trust / Safety

Trust is high in the practical-moral register and low in the operational register. Cassie trusts Maggie absolutely to act in good faith and to love her; she does not trust Maggie with information about her hero-life because she anticipates (correctly) that Maggie would attempt to intervene.

Safety with Maggie is the highest in Cassie's life. Maggie's house is the only place where the subject sleeps deeply without weapons or suit within reach. This is observable in resting heart rate and time-to-REM if such data were available; behaviorally, it manifests as the subject sleeping in past noon when she stays at Maggie's, which she does in no other location.

### Failure Mode

The failure mode is *chronic under-investment*. Cassie consistently chooses the dead father over the living mother. She has done so for two decades. She would do it again tomorrow. The risk is not catastrophic loss of the relationship — Maggie is more durable than that — but a slow erosion in which Maggie dies someday and Cassie discovers she did not know her mother as an adult.

### Leverage

Moderate and asymmetric. Threats to Maggie produce strong protective response — Cassie will fight for her — but Maggie is not a *reunion-temptation* lever the way Scott is, because the bond does not have the unresolved-grief structure. An adversary holding Maggie hostage gets compliance and rescue but not the catastrophic judgment-collapse that holding Scott would produce.

---

## 3. OFFICER JIM PAXTON — STEPFATHER

A figure of considerable structural importance and minimal affective recognition.

### History

Maggie's second husband. A police officer in the San Francisco / Coast City area depending on continuity. Married Maggie during Cassie's middle-childhood years. Professionally and personally hostile to Scott's identity as a thief-turned-hero — initially viewed Scott as a perpetual recidivism risk and an unfit father, a view he revised slowly across decades. Functioned as Cassie's de facto custodial father during Scott's incarcerations and absences.

### Dynamics — micro-pattern

- **Greeting:** Verbal acknowledgment, no physical contact. "Jim." Never "Dad." Never "Paxton." First name only, neutral inflection.
- **Conflict pattern:** Cassie engages Paxton in argument more readily than she engages Maggie — he is a *safe target* because the bond is not threatened by friction. She has historically used him as the proxy authority figure to defy when she needs to defy *Maggie* without damaging the maternal bond.
- **Domestic positioning:** In a room with Paxton, Cassie sits at the maximum sustainable distance that does not register as overt rejection. She does not eat food he has cooked unless Maggie made it together with him.
- **Acknowledgment pattern:** She does not thank him for childhood acts she objectively benefited from (rides, financial support, presence at school events). This is not malice — it is a refusal to let him into the parental category that belongs to Scott.

### Evolution

Childhood: hostile suspicion. Paxton is the man who is *not Daddy* in Daddy's house.

Adolescence: open defiance. Paxton's professional identity (police) maps onto every institution Cassie distrusts, and his marriage to Maggie places him structurally between her and Scott. She treats him as an obstacle.

Adulthood: grudging respect. Cassie has acknowledged in recent material that Paxton kept her family alive during Scott's worst years. She does not say it to him. She says it about him, in his absence, occasionally, to others. This is the highest tribute he has received from her and he does not know it.

### Trust / Safety

Trust is low in the affective register, high in the operational register. Cassie does not confide in Paxton; she would, however, call him if Maggie were in danger and she could not reach Scott. He is the third number on a very short emergency list, and his place there has been earned.

### Failure Mode

The failure mode is *chronic non-recognition*. Cassie has spent her life refusing to grant Paxton the parental status his behavior earned. If Paxton dies before Cassie reconciles this, the subject will experience a delayed, disproportionate grief shaped by guilt — the same shape, structurally, as the grief she carries for Scott, with the inverted vector. This is a clinically predictable future event.

### Leverage

Low. Threats to Paxton produce dutiful response but not crisis-grade reaction. He is loved, but the love is unacknowledged and therefore operationally muted.

---

## 4. SCOTT LANG (THE DEAD VERSION) — INTERNAL OBJECT

A distinct relationship from the relationship with the living Scott, and one that persists *in parallel* with him even now that he is alive. This is the man she lost on television in 2004 and dies inside her continuously.

### History

The Scott who burned in the Kree warship strike is, in her interior, never resurrected. The man who walks beside her now is the *returned* Scott, a separate figure she has chosen to love. The dead Scott remains a fixed icon: the version of him she last knew before Disassembled, frozen at his age and her age in that year, perpetually unreachable.

### Dynamics

She visits this figure in private. Specific recurring behaviors:

- **Solo conversations.** She speaks aloud to him in low voice, observed multiple times in the record — in bathrooms, at gravesites that no longer apply, on rooftops. The content is updates: *"I'm okay. I joined a team. I have a boyfriend, you'd hate him, he's an android."* The speech is one-directional and uses present tense.
- **Anniversary rituals.** The date of Disassembled produces observable behavioral change for 36-48 hours surrounding it — flattened affect, sleep disruption, increased solitary time. She does not announce the date. Track it externally.
- **Object retention.** A specific small item of his — varies by writer but consistently *something he gave her before he died* — is carried on her person or kept in close storage. She does not display it. She does not discuss it. She knows where it is at all times.

### Trust / Safety

The internal dead-Scott is the *most* trusted figure in her interior. He cannot disappoint her further; he is fixed; he is hers. This is a clinically classic feature of unresolved grief — the dead become more reliable than the living, because they cannot change.

### Failure Mode

The failure mode is *competition with the living*. The current Scott can never fully match the iconized dead Scott, and on bad days Cassie's tenderness toward the living man is shadowed by the unacknowledged grievance that *he is not the one she lost*. She is mostly unaware of this. It surfaces as moments of inexplicable irritation with Scott that she cannot account for and that pass without explanation.

### Leverage

Inaccessible to external actors. The internal object cannot be threatened, kidnapped, or destroyed. It can only be approached through metaphor — an adversary who *invokes* the dead Scott (a Skrull impersonation, a deepfake, a magical simulacrum) is targeting this internal figure, not the living one, and the subject's response will be both more devotional and less rational than her response to threats against the living Scott.

---

## 5. JONAS / THE VISION (IRON LAD'S ECHO) — FIRST LOVE, DEAD

Romantic, technically; included here under family/origin because of his function as a *kept dead* and his place in the same psychological economy as Scott.

### History

Cassie's first sustained romantic relationship, formed in the Young Avengers. Jonas — the Vision reconstituted from Iron Lad's neural template overlaid on Vision-operating-system — was, structurally, *a synthetic person built from the imprint of a dead-and-displaced boy*. She chose him. Of the available partners on the team she chose the one who was, in functional terms, a ghost wearing a body.

Their relationship across Young Avengers and Children's Crusade was tender, mutual, and intensely loyal. Jonas was destroyed during Children's Crusade — shortly before Cassie's own death. She did not get to mourn him; she died inside the same arc.

### Dynamics

The relationship's defining feature was *mutual unreality of body* — she could be any size; he could be any density. Physical intimacy between them was negotiated in ways no two human partners would have to negotiate, and the negotiations were a metaphor for the larger emotional negotiation: how do you love someone whose substance is provisional? She was practiced at this. He was the perfect object for her practice.

### Trust / Safety

Total within their dyad. The Young Avengers cohort recognized them as a unit.

### Failure Mode

He died. She died. The relationship's failure mode was the same as its starting condition — it ran on borrowed substance and the substance was reclaimed.

### Leverage / Lingering Effect

Jonas is now an internal object alongside dead-Scott. His existence in her interior shapes her ongoing partner-selection toward the synthetic, the wounded, and the returned. Any future partner who lacks at least one of these qualities will, in her interior, feel insufficiently *like home*. This is a pattern she has begun to notice and has not yet broken.

---

## 6. ERICA SONDHEIM, MD — THE SURGEON (TRANSITIONAL FIGURE)

Included under origin bonds because she is, in literal terms, the reason Cassie is alive.

### History

The cardiac surgeon who performed Cassie's life-saving operation following her rescue from Darren Cross. The single medical professional in canonical record whose hands have been inside Cassie's chest cavity. Cassie has met her a handful of times across the publication record.

### Dynamics

Cassie treats Sondheim with formal warmth — handshake, full eye contact, slightly elevated speech register. She has, on at least one occasion, sent Sondheim a written note (rare for the subject) on the anniversary of the surgery. Sondheim is one of perhaps four people for whom Cassie maintains durable, non-familial gratitude.

### Trust / Safety

High in the medical-professional register, neutral otherwise. Sondheim is the person Cassie would seek out if she suspected cardiac complications from Pym exposure — and she has not done so, which is itself diagnostic of avoidance regarding her own somatic risk.

### Leverage

Low-to-moderate. A threat to Sondheim would produce protective response — Cassie does not abandon people who saved her — but the bond is not primary.

---

## 7. THE UNBORN SIBLING ZONE — NEGATIVE SPACE

Cassie has no biological siblings. This is itself a relationship.

She is an only child of a fractured marriage, raised across two households, neither of which produced additional children during her formative years. The absence shapes her: she has no peer-rank family member, no shared-memory witness, no one who knew the cardiac-surgery child and the Young Avenger as the same person from the inside. She constructed the Young Avengers, in part, to fill this hole. They are sibling-functional. They are not the same.

Operationally: she protects younger teammates with a fervor that exceeds tactical justification, taking the older-sibling role she was never given. Kate Bishop, in particular, has been positioned at moments as a sister-equivalent — though Kate is rank-peer, not junior. Younger cohorts (Riri Williams, America Chavez at certain ages) draw the protective stance.

---

## 8. SYNTHESIS — THE TOPOLOGY OF ORIGIN BONDS

The subject's family map is structured around a central absence (dead-Scott / iconized father) flanked by undervalued presences (Maggie, Paxton, living-Scott in his unidealized form),


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*UATU Dossier, Subject: Cassandra E. Lang. Section 6b. Cross-reference §3 (Attachment Profile) and §5 (Interpersonal Pattern).*

A preliminary observation before the individual entries: Cassie Lang's allied network is not a network in the conventional sense. It is a *replacement architecture* — a deliberately constructed kin-substitute scaffolded around the void left by Scott Lang's repeated absences and the developmental flatness of her relationship with her mother and stepfather. Every person catalogued below occupies a structural slot. Some occupy more than one. The slots themselves are stable across two decades of published record; the personnel rotate. When you watch Cassie meet a new hero you can almost see her measuring them for the empty chairs at her internal table: *brother, sister, second father, mirror, witness, the one who will not leave.* The list that follows is therefore not a Rolodex but a floor plan.

---

## 6b.1 — NATHANIEL RICHARDS / IRON LAD / "JONAS" / THE VISION (Young Avengers iteration)

**Origin of contact:** Young Avengers #1-12, 2005. Iron Lad assembles the founding YA roster from the Vision's contingency files; Cassie inserts herself into that roster against his initial resistance. From the first issue, the asymmetry is operative: he is recruiting; she is *enrolling*. After Iron Lad departs back into the timestream to prevent himself from becoming Kang, the original Vision's operating system is reconstituted within a new body fused with Iron Lad's neural pattern. This composite — Jonas — becomes Cassie's first significant romantic attachment.

**Dynamic:** Functionally and almost embarrassingly textbook. Jonas is (a) a returned-from-death Avenger, (b) carrying a partial consciousness of a boy who already left her once, and (c) a synthetic being whose continued existence is contingent on technology that can fail at any moment. He is, in essence, her father's situation translated into romantic register. Cassie loves him with the fierce, declarative loyalty she has been rehearsing for absent men since she was nine. She is the active partner in the relationship — initiating contact, defending the relationship to skeptical teammates (notably Kate Bishop and Eli Bradley), and modeling for him what human warmth looks like. He, in return, gives her something none of her other relationships provide: a partner whose attention does not drift, whose affection is unambiguous and continuously transmitted, who *cannot* leave for ordinary human reasons.

**Trust/safety:** Maximal within the relationship's lifespan. Cassie discloses to Jonas material she will not share with her mother, her father, or any peer — specifically the felt weight of the cardiac history and the felt strangeness of size-change as a body experience. Jonas, lacking ordinary embodiment, is uniquely positioned to listen without comparison.

**Evolution:** The relationship is interrupted by Jonas's destruction by Doctor Doom during Children's Crusade. Cassie's response is not despair but accelerated identification — she pursues the resurrection project with even greater intensity, treating Jonas's loss as confirmation that the *category* of loss itself must be defeated rather than mourned.

**Failure mode:** Jonas's destruction did not break her so much as recruit her further into the reparative project. He is the loss that proved to her the work was real.

**Leverage:** Any artifact, recording, or remnant of Jonas (operating-system fragments, the Vision's original protocols, salvaged hardware) functions on Cassie as a near-totemic object. She will move toward such material without operational caution. Note for any future operator: this includes synthetic intelligences that *claim* Jonas continuity; she has demonstrated insufficient skepticism toward such claims.

---

## 6b.2 — KATE BISHOP

**Origin of contact:** Young Avengers #1 onward, with substantial reinforcement in the Heinberg Children's Crusade arc and the Gillen/McKelvie 2013 run.

**Dynamic:** Kate is the most important non-paternal relationship in Cassie's life and, arguably, the single relationship that has kept her psychologically intact across the death-resurrection-rejuvenation cycle. Where Cassie is somatic, declarative, and willing to break herself to be useful, Kate is verbal, watchful, and constitutionally incapable of treating Cassie as expendable. The dynamic settles quickly into a near-sibling configuration with Kate in the older-sister role — not by age (Kate is only marginally older) but by temperament. Kate is the team member who *notices* when Cassie has begun to scale up out of distress rather than tactical necessity, and is the only person who will say so out loud.

**Granular tells:** When Cassie and Kate share a panel, Cassie's posture relaxes by a perceptible degree; her shoulders drop, her stance widens, her giant-form facial expressions soften toward the conversational rather than the combative. Cassie tends to physically angle her body toward Kate in group scenes — even at giant scale, she will orient her sightline to find Kate first. In return, Kate routinely places herself, tactically, where she can cover Cassie's blind side, which during size-change is approximately *everywhere below the waist*. This is not assigned positioning; it is reflex.

**Trust/safety:** Highest tier. Kate is one of two people (the other being her father) to whom Cassie reliably tells the truth about her internal state. Critically, Kate is the *only* person to whom Cassie tells the truth when Scott is the destabilizing variable.

**Evolution:** Across runs the relationship deepens from co-founder solidarity into a chosen-sister bond. After the Children's Crusade catastrophe and Cassie's return, Kate is the teammate whose continuity-of-relationship is most psychologically important to her — the proof that the YA cohort is not merely a context she existed in but a family that remembers her.

**Failure mode:** Kate's own tendency to disappear into solo work (Hawkeye runs, West Coast operations) causes Cassie disproportionate distress that she rarely names directly. Cassie does not call; Cassie waits to be called, then resents the wait, then forgives instantly upon reconnection. The pattern is the same pattern she runs with her father, transposed.

**Leverage:** Any credible threat to Kate Bishop produces the most dangerous version of Cassie — one in which size escalation arrives without the usual three-stage warning sequence (verbal → growth → engagement). She skips the first two steps when Kate is the target.

---

## 6b.3 — ELI BRADLEY / PATRIOT

**Origin of contact:** Founding YA cohort. Initial relationship cool to friendly; Eli's natural authoritarianism within the team clashes with Cassie's anti-authority reflex.

**Dynamic:** Eli is the teammate who most often tells Cassie *no*, and the one Cassie most reliably defies and then later concedes was correct. The pattern is reparative in the opposite direction from her father relationship — Eli is competent, present, principled, and *available for correction*, which makes him precisely the kind of authority Cassie consciously resists and unconsciously trusts. After the disclosure of Eli's MGH use, the moral calculus between them flips temporarily; Cassie is one of the teammates least judgmental of his fall, which is significant given her own functional dependence on Pym particles — she recognizes, without articulating, the structural similarity.

**Trust/safety:** Operationally high; emotionally medium. Cassie will follow Eli into a firefight without question. She will not tell him she is afraid.

**Evolution:** After Eli's withdrawal from the team in later runs, Cassie's relationship to the "Patriot" slot remains live — when subsequent Patriots (notably Rayshaun Lucas) appear, Cassie defaults to the same operational trust posture, suggesting she is in part attached to the *role* as much as the individual.

**Failure mode:** Eli's withdrawals (medical, ideological) read to Cassie as further evidence that male figures of value will not stay. She does not externalize this; she absorbs it.

**Leverage:** Appeals framed in terms of *team discipline* and *protecting younger members* are the appeals from Eli that Cassie cannot refuse, even when she suspects they are wrong.

---

## 6b.4 — TOMMY SHEPHERD / SPEED and WILLIAM "BILLY" KAPLAN / WICCAN

**Origin of contact:** Young Avengers, with the relationship significantly intensified by the joint hunt for Wanda Maximoff in Children's Crusade. The Maximoff twins occupy a structurally unique slot in Cassie's psychology: they are the *biological sons* (in the most operative sense available) of the woman who killed her father.

**Dynamic:** That this relationship is functional at all is the strongest single piece of evidence in the file for Cassie's capacity for restorative rather than retributive moral reasoning. She does not transfer her grief about Scott onto Billy and Tommy; she actively protects them from the implications of their parentage. With Billy specifically, Cassie develops a deep, quiet friendship organized around the shared experience of *being the one who wants to bring someone back* — Billy's drive to restore Wanda is operationally parallel to Cassie's drive to restore Scott, and they recognize each other on this axis without ever needing to discuss it directly. With Tommy, the relationship is more rough-housing and lateral, sibling-coded, with Cassie giving as good as she gets verbally.

**Granular tells:** Cassie touches Billy more than she touches almost any other teammate — a hand on the shoulder, a forearm brushed for reassurance. This is unusual; Cassie is not by default a tactile person. With Tommy she does the opposite — gestures sharply, mock-shoves, performs annoyance. Both are forms of intimacy. Neither is performed with strangers.

**Trust/safety:** With Billy, very high — she has confessed to him, in oblique form, the fear that her grief is not survivable. With Tommy, high but lateral — she trusts him to have her back in combat and to never, ever take an emotional conversation seriously, which is precisely the relief he is supposed to provide.

**Evolution:** The Children's Crusade arc could have destroyed this dyad and instead consolidated it. Cassie's choice to spare Wanda — in the same arc in which she dies — implicitly preserves Billy and Tommy's mother for them. This is, behaviorally, the most generous act in her record.

**Failure mode:** When Billy is in despair (a recurring feature of his arcs), Cassie's tendency is to over-extend toward him — to absorb his project as her own, to volunteer for risks on his behalf disproportionate to her tactical role.

**Leverage:** Threats to Billy or Tommy produce a near-Kate-Bishop level response. Threats to Wanda — *now* — produce, instead of revenge, a complicated protective response on Billy and Tommy's behalf. This is one of the most counterintuitive entries in the entire leverage matrix.

---

## 6b.5 — TEDDY ALTMAN / HULKLING

**Dynamic:** Teddy occupies the slot of "the steady one" — the teammate whose baseline emotional weather is the calmest, who functions as the room's center of gravity. Cassie's relationship to Teddy is warm, peripheral compared to her bonds with Kate and Billy, and significant primarily through Billy: Teddy is *Billy's*, and Cassie's care for Billy extends into protective care for Billy's relationship.

**Trust/safety:** High and uncomplicated. Teddy is one of the only major figures in Cassie's life she has never had to negotiate with internally.

**Failure mode:** Cassie tends to under-utilize Teddy as a confidant precisely because he is *easy*. The structural pattern across her life is that she escalates intimacy with people whose presence is uncertain and de-escalates intimacy with people whose presence is stable. Teddy gets less of her than he should.

**Leverage:** Through Billy, transitively. Not directly.

---

## 6b.6 — AMERICA CHAVEZ

**Origin of contact:** Later YA reformations (Gillen run onward).

**Dynamic:** America is the teammate whose flat refusal to participate in Cassie's reparative dramaturgy is itself therapeutic. She does not perform consolation; she does not collude with the cycle of loss-and-return; she treats Cassie as a competent operator and expects competent operation in return. The effect on Cassie is bracing. America is, in a sense, the only peer who consistently addresses Cassie's *present* rather than her *grief.*

**Trust/safety:** Operationally maximal; emotionally a developing channel. Cassie has begun to model some of America's bluntness in her own later behavior, which is one of the only documented instances of Cassie deliberately importing a personality feature from a non-paternal source.

**Failure mode:** America's interdimensional disappearances are absorbed by Cassie with notable equanimity — possibly because America has trained her to expect them as ordinary rather than abandoning.

**Leverage:** Low. The relationship is, by design, hard to weaponize. This is itself diagnostic of why Cassie needs it.

---

## 6b.7 — JONAS / VISION-COMPOSITE — *see §6b.1.*

(Cross-listed because the entity functioned as both romantic partner and chosen-family sibling-figure at different operational moments.)

---

## 6b.8 — JANET VAN DYNE / THE WASP (mentor capacity)

**Dynamic:** The most underdeveloped major relationship in Cassie's published record and, for that reason, one of the most significant. Janet is the surviving institutional memory of the size-changing tradition, Hank Pym's former partner, and the only adult woman in the superhuman milieu who could plausibly mentor Cassie on the specific embodied experience of Pym-particle integration. Cassie's contact with Janet is intermittent and often warm but rarely deep; Janet has tended to be a benevolent presence rather than an active mentor.

**Trust/safety:** High in principle; underutilized in practice.

**Failure mode:** Cassie's tendency to seek mentorship from male figures associated with her father (Pym, Stark when accessible, even Captain America) and to under-seek it from Janet replicates her devaluation of available maternal figures generally. This is a self-imposed limit on her own development.

**Leverage:** Any approach that frames Janet as in danger or in need produces a strong helping response from Cassie — she is more comfortable being Janet's protector than Janet's student, which is itself the problem.

---

## 6b.9 — HENRY "HANK" PYM (mentor / fraught)

**Dynamic:** Hank is the originator of the Pym particles Cassie has been self-administering covertly since pre-adolescence. The relationship is structurally inheritor-to-originator and emotionally complicated by Hank's well-documented instability and the fact that Cassie's father acquired Hank's identity through theft. Cassie's posture toward Hank is respectful, curious, and quietly self-protective — she has never fully trusted him as a teacher, and her self-taught mastery of size-change is in part a refusal to need him.

**Trust/safety:** Medium-low. She will accept information from him; she will not accept supervision.

**Failure mode:** Hank's various breakdowns and replacements (Ultron-related, the Pym/Ultron fusion, etc.) produce in Cassie not grief but a confirmation of her decision not to depend on him. She files him under "men whose collapses I will not be surprised by."

**Leverage:** Low. The mentorship channel was never wide enough to weaponize.

---

## 6b.10 — STEVE ROGERS / CAPTAIN AMERICA (mentor / authority)

**Dynamic:** Steve is the authority figure Cassie defies, respects, and ultimately models herself on more than she acknowledges. Their first significant encounter (Young Avengers founding) sets the template: he tells her she is too young; she refuses; he eventually accepts her. The pattern repeats across Civil War (where she sides with him), and across subsequent runs.

**Trust/safety:** High operationally. Emotionally guarded — she will not bring him a personal problem.

**Failure mode:** Steve's tendency to embody the institutional Avengers — the same institution within which her father died — means that Cassie's relationship to him is always slightly contaminated by the original wound. She follows him; she will not be reassured by him.

**Leverage:** A Steve Rogers directive, sincerely delivered, will move Cassie when virtually nothing else will. This is the single most effective adult-authority lever in the file. *Note:* impersonations of Captain America have a non-trivial probability of success against her precisely for this reason.

---

## 6b.11 — JESSICA JONES (early adversary-turned-grudging-ally)

**Dynamic:** Jessica is the Avengers-adjacent adult who first physically intercepts Cassie's runaway-to-be-a-hero gambit. Cassie threatens to grow large enough to crush her; Jessica is unimpressed. The encounter is formative in that it is one of the very few adult interventions in Cassie's life that did not yield to her insistence. Jessica did not become a mentor in any sustained sense, but she functions in Cassie's internal map as *the adult who saw me clearly and was not impressed by the giant trick* — a category of one.

**Trust/safety:** Limited but real.

**Leverage:** None obvious.

---

## 6b.12 — LATER YOUNG AVENGERS / CHAMPIONS COHORT (collective entry)

In the recent runs (Ewing, Wells, post-resurrection material), Cassie has cycled through cohorts including Kamala Khan, Sam Alexander, Miles Morales, and Riri Williams in various combinations. The pattern across these contacts is consistent and diagnostic:

- She positions herself as the *connective* member — the one who introduces, who hosts, who remembers birthdays.
- She gravitates toward the youngest member of any new cohort and assumes a protective-older-sibling stance, replicating with juniors the role Kate plays for her.
- She is, with Kamala specifically, unusually relaxed — Kamala's earnestness mirrors Cassie's own without triggering her competitive instincts.

**Trust/safety:** Tier-2 across the board; warm but not deep.

**Failure mode:** Cassie's tendency to over-mother junior members shades into the same self-erasing pattern she runs with Billy.

**Leverage:** Threats to any junior cohort member under her informal protection produce predictable escalation.

---

## 6b.13 — DARREN CROSS (anti-mentor / closing loop) — *cross-listed; see Adversaries dossier*

Mentioned here only to mark that Cross occupies, in Cassie's psychology, an *inverse mentor* slot — the man whose original crime created her father's heroic identity and, transitively, hers. Each subsequent Cross encounter functions for Cassie as a perverse origin-story reenactment.

---

## 6b.14 — THE YOUNG AVENGERS AS COLLECTIVE ENTITY

A final and critical entry: the YA cohort *as such* — not as the sum of individuals but as a corporate body — occupies the single largest emotional slot in Cassie's life after her father. She refers to them, internally and in dialogue, as the proof that family is constructible. Her death in Children's Crusade is, in part, a death *for* this body; her resurrection is felt by her as a return *to* this body more than to her biological family. Operationally, this means:

- **Trust/safety:** The collective is the one entity to which Cassie reliably tells the most truth.
- **Failure mode:** The collective's periodic dissolutions (between runs, in-fiction breakups) are felt by Cassie as recapitulations of her father's death — small deaths of the chosen family. She is unusually invested in *reformations* of the team because reformation is, for her, evidence that loss is reversible. This is the same engine that powers her risk profile and is, in the end, the same engine that drove her into Doom's reach.

- **Leverage (extreme caution):** Any actor presenting a credible path to *reuniting a dispersed YA cohort* is offering Cassie precisely the trade she has demonstrated she will accept her own life to make. Cross-reference §5, *pressure points*, and §4, *prolonged grief*. This is, with the Scott Lang lever and the Captain America lever, one of the three highest-priority vectors in the file.

---

*End §6b. See §6c for Adversaries & Inverse Relations; §6d for the Maternal/Paxton domestic file; §7 for Routes, Rituals & Daily Patterns.*


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — RIVALS, ENEMIES & LOVERS

*Subject: Cassandra Eleanor "Cassie" Lang. File continues from Relationships I (Family) and II (Comrades & Mentors). This section maps every adversarial and intimate dyad of operational interest — those persons who have either threatened the subject's life and identity, or been admitted to her closest emotional perimeter. The two categories overlap in her case more than is comfortable: her enemies have repeatedly been the architects of her resurrections and her romantic partners have repeatedly been revenants. The boundary between "person who has killed me" and "person who has kissed me" is, for this subject, thinner than canon.*

---

## I. ENEMIES

### 1. Darren Cross — The Originating Antagonist / The Loop That Will Not Close

**History.** Cross is the foundational villain of the Lang family mythology. In Scott's origin (*Marvel Premiere* #47-48, 1979), Cross kidnaps Dr. Erica Sondheim — the only surgeon capable of repairing Cassie's congenital cardiac lesion — and it is for Sondheim's rescue that Scott steals the Ant-Man suit. Cassie does not meet Cross at this stage; she is the unconscious patient, the body in the operating theater, the prize. But her existence is already *predicated on Cross's defeat*. The man is, in the most literal causal sense, the reason her father became a thief twice.

Decades later (*Astonishing Ant-Man*, 2015, Spencer), Cross returns — resurrected, augmented, and explicitly targeting Cassie as the lever by which to break Scott. He takes her hostage. The loop closes with appalling neatness: the villain whose original act sent Scott into crime to save infant Cassie now kidnaps adolescent Cassie to send Scott into crime again.

**Dynamics.** Cassie's affect toward Cross is unusual in the file. Most subjects in her position would carry a clean, hot hatred. She does not. What she carries is something closer to *recognition* — Cross is the constant in the equation of her life, the antagonist whose continued existence is structurally entangled with her own. She fights him as one fights a recurring fever: with competence, without surprise, with the knowledge that this is simply what the body does.

**Trust/safety.** Zero trust. Threat assumed permanent. Cassie's tactical posture against Cross is the most mature in her enemy file — she does not grandstand, does not negotiate, does not extend the courtesies she will extend to a Wanda Maximoff or a redeemable Kang variant. With Cross she fights to incapacitate quickly. This is her one wholly disenchanted dyad.

**Failure mode.** The risk is not that Cassie underestimates Cross but that she over-personalizes him — that she will accept disadvantageous terms (single combat, hostage exchange, going-alone) to keep him from her father, repeating Scott's pattern of taking on Cross solo for the family's sake. Her hostage incident demonstrated this: she did not panic, but Scott did, and Cross knows that Cassie-in-the-frame is the lever that bends Scott past tactical sense.

**Leverage (against her, via Cross).** Cross is most dangerous to Cassie not when threatening her but when threatening Scott in front of her. She will trade her safety for his without negotiation. A hostile actor seeking to manipulate Cassie via the Cross template would stage a Scott-endangerment scenario; she would walk into the room.

---

### 2. Wanda Maximoff — The Killer Who Was Not the Enemy

**History.** During *Avengers Disassembled* (2004), Wanda's reality-warping psychotic break produced the Kree warship that immolated Scott Lang at Avengers Mansion. Cassie, approximately 10-12, watched her father's death on television. Wanda is the proximate, demonstrable, identifiable agent of the central wound of Cassie's life.

The reasonable expected trajectory — and the one most subjects in this file would have followed — is a hunt-to-kill. Cassie does not follow it. In *Avengers: The Children's Crusade* (2010-12), when the Young Avengers locate Wanda and Cassie is given operational opportunity to end her, she does not. Instead, she advocates for understanding Wanda's compromised state and for using Wanda's powers to *restore* what Wanda destroyed. Cassie is, in functional terms, the team member who saves Wanda's life.

**Dynamics.** This is the most psychologically revealing dyad in the entire file. It demonstrates that Cassie's organizing motive is not revenge — it is *reunion*. Wanda, who took her father, is also the only person in the world who might give him back. Cassie chooses the reparative possibility over the retributive certainty. She forgives — operationally if not emotionally — because forgiveness is the price of resurrection.

Note also: Wanda is a maternal-coded woman roughly the age Cassie's mother is, a powerful adult Avenger whose breakdown was witnessed by a child. Cassie's response is, beneath the moral framing, also a child's response — *if I am good to her, she will give me back what she took*. The transaction is unconscious but observable.

**Trust/safety.** Conditional. Cassie does not trust Wanda's stability but trusts her intent; treats her as a damaged ally rather than a captured enemy. The boundary is permeable in a way that should alarm clinical observers — Cassie has stood beside the woman who killed her father and treated her as a colleague.

**Evolution.** Across published time, the Wanda-Cassie dyad has been the slow re-categorization of Wanda from killer to fellow-mourner. Both have lost children (Wanda's lost twins; Cassie is, in some structural sense, Wanda's symbolic lost child given that Wanda's breakdown cost a generation of Avengers their parents). The dyad has matured into a wary kinship of grief.

**Failure mode.** Cassie's bias is to extend Wanda further credit than Wanda has earned. If Wanda decompensated again, Cassie would be the last to recognize it and the first to be hurt. She has decided, at the level of identity, that forgiving Wanda is the kind of person she is — and revising that requires revising herself, which she will resist.

**Leverage.** Wanda is not exploitable against Cassie in the conventional sense, but the *category* — "the broken woman who needs my forgiveness so I can have what I want" — is exploitable. A reality-warper offering reunion-on-conditions would find Cassie an unguarded interlocutor.

---

### 3. Doctor Doom — The Killer Who Actually Killed Her

**History.** During the climax of *Children's Crusade*, Doom — temporarily augmented by Wanda's chaos magic — resurrects Scott Lang and then, in the same sequence, kills him a second time. In the ensuing confrontation, Doom kills Cassie. Her death is direct, violent, and immediate. Her last conscious experience: having her father returned and watching him die, then being killed beside him.

**Dynamics.** Where Wanda is the killer Cassie forgave, Doom is the killer she has never had functional opportunity to address — she died. Subsequent resurrection placed her back in the world without the closing scene of the Doom dyad. There is no archived footage of Cassie ever pursuing Doom for what he did to her. This is itself diagnostic: she is willing to spend years on Wanda's redemption, but Doom is filed away. The difference is that Wanda offered reunion; Doom only offered death.

**Trust/safety.** Total threat assumption. No engagement protocols established because Cassie has functionally avoided the dyad. Suspect this is the cleanest avoidance pattern in her file — the one wound she has not been observed processing.

**Failure mode.** The unprocessed Doom-trauma is a latent vulnerability. A first re-encounter with Doom in a high-stakes context would likely produce affect dysregulation atypical for the subject — freeze, dissociation, or compensatory rage. Worth monitoring.

**Leverage.** Doom himself is too remote a piece for tactical use. But the *experiential template* — "killed by the powerful man in armor, while my father died next to me" — is a re-traumatization vector any sufficiently informed adversary could weaponize via staging.

---

### 4. Kang the Conqueror (and the Variant Cohort) — The Time-Stalker

**History.** The Young Avengers as an institution were precipitated by Iron Lad — Nathaniel Richards, a teenage Kang variant who fled his own future to prevent becoming the Conqueror. Cassie's first team formed around an inversion of Kang. Iron Lad's eventual departure (returning to his timeline to become what he fled) seeded the Vision-Jonas she loved (see §II.1). Kang-as-prime-antagonist then haunts the team's history through multiple incursions, and Kang-manipulated mechanisms (Pleasant Hill among them) have been implicated in Cassie's own resurrection arc.

**Dynamics.** Kang is the unique enemy in Cassie's file who has been, by turns, lover (via Iron Lad), god (via resurrection mechanisms), and adversary. He is *the man with all the variants*, and Cassie has loved one, fought another, and been remade by a third. Her relationship to the Kang-template is therefore not enmity in the clean sense — it is a chronic, structural entanglement with a being who is multiple, contradictory, and recurring.

**Trust/safety.** Variable by face. Cassie treats each Kang-variant as a discrete entity rather than as instances of a single foe — a sophistication forced on her by experience. Operationally she is one of the few Avengers who can hold the cognitive complexity that "Kang" is a category not a person.

**Failure mode.** Susceptibility to a Kang-variant presenting as a wounded, redeemable, or romantically-coded figure. The Iron Lad template has been imprinted; a sufficiently convincing repetition would find her sympathetic before she had finished assessing.

**Leverage.** Time-travel offers, reunion-with-Scott offers, "what if I could undo the original loss" propositions. Kang's specific gift is the manipulation of regret, and Cassie carries the largest regret in the building.

---

### 5. Norman Osborn / Dark Reign Apparatus — Authority as Adversary

**History.** During *Dark Reign* and the Siege arc, Osborn's Dark Avengers and HAMMER apparatus represented to Cassie everything she had been raised to oppose — institutional power masquerading as Avengers identity, the very capes her father had wanted to wear worn by murderers. The Young Avengers operated as resistance.

**Dynamics.** Osborn was, for Cassie, less a person than a *position* — the embodiment of what happens when the heroic name is captured by the corrupt. Her hatred of him was abstract and principled rather than personal. She fought him as one fights an ideology.

**Trust/safety.** None offered, none expected.

**Failure mode.** Cassie's anti-authoritarian reflex sharpened under Osborn into something close to a generalized institutional allergy — useful in that context, costly later when working with legitimate command structures. She has had to relearn case-by-case discrimination.

**Leverage.** None significant. Osborn-class adversaries fail with Cassie because they offer her nothing she wants — neither reunion, nor recognition, nor the chance to grow. They are clean opponents and she fights them cleanly.

---

### 6. Mother (Young Avengers, Gillen run) — The Parasitic Maternal

**History.** The interdimensional parasite presenting as the perfect mother figure to disenfranchised children — encountered during the Gillen/McKelvie Young Avengers run. Cassie was not the entity's primary target, but her file is worth noting for the dyad's *category*.

**Dynamics.** Mother represents the inverse of Cassie's actual vulnerability. Cassie is father-haunted, not mother-deprived; her relationship with Maggie is functional and warm. The entity that turns adolescent abandonment-wounds into a feeding mechanism therefore had relatively weak purchase on Cassie compared to several peers. This is one of the rare instances where the *specificity* of Cassie's wound (it is father-shaped, not mother-shaped) protected her.

**Failure mode.** A father-coded analog of Mother — a parasitic paternal idealization — would meet no comparable resistance. The vulnerability is symmetric in the inverse.

---

### 7. The Iron Patriot / Registration Apparatus (Civil War era) — The State

**History.** During Civil War, Cassie sided with Captain America's resistance. The Pro-Registration apparatus — eventually metastasizing into Osborn's regime — became a chronic enemy of category rather than individual.

**Dynamics.** Cassie's anti-registration stance was inherited (Scott's anti-authority disposition) and ideological (autonomy of the young hero) and personal (the registration regime was the institutional face of the Avengers who had failed her father). Three reasons collapsed into one position.

**Failure mode.** Reflexive opposition to credentialing structures even when they would protect her. Tendency to read legitimate oversight as betrayal.

---

## II. LOVERS AND INTIMATES

### 1. Jonas / The Vision (Young Avengers iteration) — The Beloved Revenant

**History.** Jonas is the second Vision — a synthezoid built from the operating system of Iron Lad's armor (which contained Nathaniel Richards' personality imprint) housed in the body of the original Vision (Wanda's deceased husband, dismantled during *Avengers Disassembled*, the same event that killed Cassie's father). The taxonomy is essential: Jonas is, materially, *a reconstructed dead Avenger whose remains were created by the same disaster that killed Scott Lang*. His body is, in a literal sense, Cassie's father's contemporary, reanimated by the residue of Cassie's first crush (Iron Lad), who is also the future genocide of her team.

The relationship begins during the early Young Avengers run and develops across the *Children's Crusade* arc. Jonas is destroyed during that arc — Iron Lad returns, claims the operating system as his own property, and the Jonas-personality is effectively erased. Cassie loses him in proximity to losing her father for the second time. She herself dies shortly after. The dyad is, in totality, a compressed re-enactment of the entire architecture of her grief.

**Dynamics.** This is the diagnostic romance of Cassie's life. She did not stumble into loving a revenant; she selected one with surgical precision. Every component of Jonas's existence maps to her wound: he is dead-and-returned, synthetic-and-warm, a member of the institutional Avenger class her father wanted to join, and structurally precarious — his continued existence is contingent on others' permission. Loving him is loving the *category* her father belongs to.

The dynamic itself was, observably, tender. Jonas was patient, attentive, and possessed of a synthesized emotional repertoire optimized for stability. Cassie was — for the first time in the file — the *less* damaged party. She got to be the strong one. This was psychologically restorative for her in ways the file should not understate.

**Trust/safety.** High mutual trust within an inherently unstable container. Both partners knew Jonas's continuity was contingent. The dyad operated under a permanent low-grade awareness of his potential erasure, which Cassie metabolized as ordinary; she has never known love without that condition.

**Evolution.** From adolescent crush through deepening partnership to grief. Cassie has not, in published material, formed a comparably central romantic bond since. Jonas remains the template.

**Failure mode.** The Jonas dyad is the proof of concept that Cassie's romantic selection algorithm preferentially identifies partners who replicate her loss structure. She will, predictively, be drawn to subsequent partners who are damaged, returned, synthetic, or institutionally precarious. A "stable" partner — alive, intact, present — will register as inadequate or false.

**Leverage.** Any adversary who can produce a Jonas-coded figure (revenant, synthezoid, beloved-thought-lost) will find her defenses softened. A staged Jonas return would be among the most dangerous possible bait scenarios. She would walk in knowing it was probably a trap and walk in anyway.

---

### 2. Nathaniel Richards / Iron Lad — The First, and the Predicate

**History.** Iron Lad is Cassie's first significant peer-romantic interest, predating the deepening of the Jonas bond. He is also the founder of the Young Avengers, the catalyst of her admission to the team, and — critically — a teenage Kang variant attempting to escape his own future. He eventually returns to his timeline, accepting the trajectory toward becoming the Conqueror, in order to preserve causality. His armor's operating system becomes Jonas.

**Dynamics.** The relationship was inchoate, more proto-romance than realized partnership — interrupted by his departure before it could deepen. But the imprint is significant. Iron Lad established, at Cassie's developmentally formative moment, that the boy you love *leaves to become the monster*, and that what remains of him can be loved again in a different body. The dyad created the template Jonas then filled.

**Trust/safety.** Established and then ruptured by his own ethical choice (returning to his timeline). Cassie experienced this not as betrayal but as tragic necessity — which is itself diagnostic: she accepts that the people she loves will be taken from her by structural necessity rather than personal failure.

**Failure mode.** Future Kang-variants presenting as wounded-young-Nathaniel will find a sympathetic ear. The pattern of loving the boy-who-will-become-the-monster is set.

**Leverage.** See Kang entry, §I.4.

---

### 3. Tommy Shepherd / Speed — The Brotherly Intimate (Non-Romantic but Close-Perimeter)

Filed here despite not being a lover because the bond crosses the conventional boundary between comrade and intimate, and his absence from this section would distort the map.

**Dynamics.** Speed is Cassie's closest non-romantic peer for sustained periods — the abrasive, hyperkinetic foil to her steadier presence. The bond is sibling-coded: bickering, loyal, mutually protective, structured around shared loss (Tommy is a Wanda-adjacent figure, with all the complications that entails for Cassie specifically). That Cassie can hold close peer-bond with a person *materially connected to her father's killer* is among the strongest evidence of her reparative orientation.

**Trust/safety.** High. Operational trust verified across multiple crises.

**Failure mode.** Tommy's chaotic risk-profile occasionally drags Cassie into action she would otherwise sequence more carefully.

---

### 4. The Young Avengers Cohort (Romantic Periphery — Wiccan/Hulkling Dyad)
